Two Modes of Knowledge of Essence: First and Second Inquiry
Author distinguishes knowledge of essence by itself and by its properties, defining two types of inquiry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the first and second types of inquiry and identify which type of definition (hadd or rasm) corresponds to each.

Core Idea
Two types of inquiry yield two types of definitions: one reveals the essence, the other reveals a property.

In this passage, the author explains that knowledge of an essence can be either direct (by itself) or indirect (by its properties). This distinction is crucial for understanding the two basic forms of definition: essential definition (hadd) and descriptive definition (rasm). We will walk through the examples of heat and the originated world to see how each inquiry works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two ways of knowing an essence
Read the passage carefully and note the phrase 'knowledge of the essence may be according to itself or according to what is attached to it'. The first way grasps the thing's specific reality; the second grasps its concomitants or attributes.
This is the epistemological foundation for the two types of definition.
2
Recognize the examples given
The author uses 'heat' (al-harara) as an example of first-inquiry knowledge: we know heat by sense perception, which captures its essence. The 'originated' (al-muhdath) is an example of second-inquiry knowledge: we know the world is originated by proofs, but we do not thereby know the quiddity of 'the originated' itself, only its property of coming from non-existence.
Heat is known by its own reality; the originated is known by its relation to its cause.
3
Map the examples to the two questions
When asked 'What is heat?' the answer gives its essence (the first question). When asked 'What is the originated?' the answer gives a property (the second question). The author calls these 'al-su'al al-awwal' and 'al-su'al al-thani'.
The first question yields a hadd; the second yields a rasm.
4
Apply to composite essences
The author states that the same two types of inquiry apply to composite essences as well. For any essence, the demand is either for its true nature or for a distinguishing property. This will be fully treated in the sections on hadd and rasm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فذلك لا يكون بين اللُّزوم لملزومه إلَّا أن يحضر في الذهن وذلك يعرف بالتأمل. ثم العلم بالماهية بسيطة كانت الماهية أو مركبة قد يكون بحسب ذاتها وقد لا يكون بل يكون بحسب ما يتعلق بها. فتكون معلومةً إذن: إمَّا بذاتها وإمَّا بتوابعها وصفاتها. فإنا إذا علمنا الحرارة بالحس مثلا فذلك العلم بالشَّيء من حيث حقيقته المخصوصة وماهيته المعيَّنة، وهو كمال درجات العلم عند الأكثر. أما إذا علمنا أنَّ العالم حادث بالدلائل الدالة عليه فلا نعلم أنَّ المحدث ما هو، وإن كان معلوما بأن العالم حادث بإحداثه، ولو كان كذلك لكان المطلوب في السُّؤال عن الماهية البسيطة: إمَّا هو الأول منهما كما إذا سئل عن الحرارة فيقال ما يدركه الإنسان بحسّ اللمس عند مماسة جرم النَّار، وإمَّا السؤال الثاني كما إذا سئل عن المحدث فيقال المحدث هو الذي لأجله انتقل الشَّيء من العدم إلى الوجود. وعلى هذا في المركَّب إذ المطلوب إمَّا هو الأول منهما أو الثاني. وذلك يعرف بتمامه في التعريفات الحدية والرسمية من بعد إن شاء الله تعالى.
That is not a necessary relation between a necessary thing and its necessary unless it is present in the mind, and that is known by reflection. Then knowledge of the essence, whether the essence is simple or composite, may be according to itself or may not be, rather according to what is attached to it. So it becomes known, then: either by itself or by its concomitants and attributes. For if we know heat by sense, for example, that is knowledge of the thing in terms of its specific reality and determinate essence, and it is the perfection of degrees of knowledge according to most. As for if we know that the world is temporally originated by the evidences indicating it, we do not know what the originated is, even if it is known that the world is originated by its originator; and if it were so, then the demand in the question about the simple essence would be either the first of the two, as when heat is asked about and it is said: what the human perceives by the sense of touch upon contact with the body of fire; or the second question, as when the originated is asked about and it is said: the originated is that for which the thing transfers from non-existence to existence. And likewise for the composite, for the demand is either the first or the second. And that is known completely in the definitions, hadd and rasm, later, God willing.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and epistemological discussion. The author first states that a necessary relation (luzum) between a thing and its necessary is not realized unless the thing is present in the mind. Then he distinguishes two ways of knowing an essence: either by its own self (its true quiddity) or by its properties and attributes. The former is exemplified by knowing heat through sense perception—this is the highest degree of knowledge because it grasps the thing's specific reality. The latter is like knowing that the world is temporally originated (hadith) through proofs; this knowledge does not reveal the quiddity of 'the originated' itself, only that it has the property of being originated. Consequently, when we ask 'what is it?' about a simple essence, there are two possible answers: the first gives the essence (like heat defined by its sensible effect), and the second gives the property (like the originated defined by its passage from non-existence). The same applies to composite essences. The author promises full treatment of these in the later sections on definition by genus and differentia (hadd) and definition by property and accident (rasm).

Key Terms
الماهية
السؤال الأول
السؤال الثاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The second inquiry (al-su'al al-thani)
C) Neither, since sense perception is not a form of knowledge
D) Both inquiries apply equally

Key Takeaway
An essence can be known either directly by its own reality (first inquiry) or indirectly by its properties (second inquiry), forming the basis for hadd and rasm definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Priority of Genus over Species and Essential Nature
Author explains the priority of existence between animal and human and the essentiality of genus for species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the genus is essential to the species and not acquired from an external cause, using the example of animal and man.

Core Idea
The genus is not added from outside; it is part of the quiddity.

This passage from the great logic text clarifies a crucial point: the relationship between genus and species is not accidental but essential. The author, commenting on Ibn Sina, argues that when we say 'man is animal', animality is not a property conferred by an external cause; rather, man is animal by his very essence. Understanding this priority is key to correct definition in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sides: predicate vs. part
The author distinguishes 'animal as predicate' (in a proposition) from 'animal as part' (of the definition). When we say 'man is animal', animal is a predicate, but it is also a part of man's quiddity. This distinction is crucial for understanding priority.
2
Grasp the three things known with the universal
When you know a universal like animal, you know (a) that the particular (man) necessarily has it; (b) that it is constitutive; (c) that it is not acquired from an external cause.
3
Follow the reductio argument
If animality were caused, then removing the cause would allow man to be without animality, which is impossible. This shows animality is essential, not accidental.
4
Distinguish essential from concomitant
The author contrasts animality with oddness for three: oddness follows the quiddity but is not constitutive. Animality is constitutive, while oddness is a necessary concomitant but not part of the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على وجود الحيوان المحمول، وإن كان وجود الحيوان الجزء متقدما على وجود الإنسان. ولما كان كلام الشَّيخ فى الحيوان المحمول، لا جرم جعل وجود الإنسان متقدما على وجود الحيوان وليرجع من يريد الإطناب في تحقيق هذا الأصل إلى المقالة الأولى من برهان الشفاء. وقد بين هذه المسألة وفصلها في من المنطق الكبير (و ۲۱ ظ): ثمَّ الَّذي لا يعلم مع ما هو من جزئياته إلَّا وأن يعلم معه ثلاثة أشياء: أحدها أنَّ الجزئي له ذلك المعنى بالضرورة. وثانيها أنَّ الذاتي مقوم على ما هو ذاتي له من الجزئيات. وثالثها أنَّه لا يكون مستفادًا من الغير. فإن الإنسان لا يكون حيوانا بالسبب الخارجي الذي جعله حيوانا بل لذاته هو حيوان؛ إذ لو كان لعلَّة لأمكن فرضه إنسانًا غير حيوان عند فرض عدم العلة. وليس هذا مصيرًا إلى أنَّ الحيوان وجد بذاته من غير علة أو جدته بل المراد أن شيئًا ما لم يجعل الإنسان حيوانا. ثمَّ الَّذي جعل الإنسان فقد جعل الحيوان لجعله الإنسان؛ لأنَّ الإنسان حيوان ما، لكن لا يمكن أن يجعل الإنسان ثم إفادة الحيوانية وإلا لكانت الإنسانيّة مقوّمة دون الحيوانية، ثم الحيوانية تتكون واردة عليه من خارج، وذلك محال، وهذا من جملة ما يشاركه فيه اللوازم التي يلزم الشَّيء لماهيته لا في وجوده كالفرديَّة للثلاثة مثلا . فإن الفرديَّة لها لا يكون بعلة بل هي في نفسها وماهيتها لا تكون إلا فردا، ففرق بين أن يوجد الشيء شيئًا وبين أن يوجد لشيء؛ إذ المفهوم من قولنا يوجده لشيء أن يوجد ذلك الشَّيء أو لا . ثم يفيد ذلك الشيء الآخر، والمفهوم من ذلك القول لا يكون كذلك، وهذا ظاهر.
Concerning the existence of animal as predicate, even though the existence of animal as part is prior to the existence of man. Since the Master's discussion was about animal as predicate, he necessarily made the existence of man prior to the existence of animal. Let whoever wishes to elaborate on verifying this principle refer to the first article of the Demonstration of the Shifa'. He has explained and elaborated on this issue in his Mantiq al-Kabir (folio 21 verso): Then, that which is not known along with what is among its particulars except that three things are known with it: first, that the particular necessarily has that meaning; second, that the essential (dhati) is constitutive of what is essential to the particulars; third, that it is not acquired from another. For man is not animal by an external cause that made him animal, but by his essence he is animal; for if it were by a cause, it would be possible to suppose him man without animal when the cause is supposed absent. This does not lead to the conclusion that animal exists by itself without a cause or its existence, but rather the meaning is that nothing made man animal. Then, that which made man has made animal by making man; because man is a certain animal, but it is impossible to make man and then confer animality, otherwise humanity would be constitutive without animality, then animality would come into existence from outside, which is impossible. This is among the things that share with the concomitants that accompany a thing for its quiddity not for its existence, like oddness for three, for example. For oddness is not by a cause, but in itself and its quiddity it is only odd. So there is a difference between 'something exists as something' and 'something exists for something'; for the meaning of our saying 'it exists for something' is that that thing exists or not, then that other thing benefits from it, and the meaning of that statement is not the same, and this is clear.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage discusses the priority of existence between genus (animal) and species (man) and the essential nature of the genus for the species. The author begins by noting that when the Master (Ibn Sina) speaks of 'animal as predicate', he treats man's existence as prior to animal's existence, even though animal as a part of man's definition is prior in terms of conceptual priority. This is because the predicate exists in the subject after the subject's existence. The author then refers to his larger work for further detail. He then states three things known when one knows a thing with its particulars: (1) the particular necessarily has that universal meaning; (2) the essential (dhati) is constitutive of the particulars; (3) the essential is not acquired from an external cause. He illustrates with the example of man being animal essentially, not by an external cause. If it were by a cause, then one could suppose man existing without animal when the cause is not present, which is absurd. This does not mean animal has no cause at all, but that nothing caused man to be animal—it is essential. Next, he argues that the cause that makes man also makes animal, because man is a kind of animal; you cannot make man first and then attach animality from outside, as that would make humanity constitutive without animality, and animality would be added, which is impossible. This is similar to necessary concomitants (lawazim) that follow the quiddity, not the existence, like oddness for the number three: oddness is not caused but is part of its essence. Finally, he distinguishes between 'a thing exists as something' (essential) and 'a thing exists for something' (accidental). In the first, the thing itself has that attribute essentially; in the second, the attribute is conferred from outside.

ذاتي
مقوم

Key Takeaway
The genus is an essential part of the species' quiddity, not an accidental addition from an external cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identifying Terms in Categorical Propositions
Author defines the parts of a proposition and terms with an example syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the terms (hudud) in a categorical proposition and distinguish them from quantifiers, copula, and modality.

Core Idea
The terms of a proposition are its subject and predicate — nothing more.

When you break down a categorical proposition to its logical bones, what remains? Only the subject and the predicate. In this lesson, you will learn what counts as a 'term' (hadd) and how it fits into a syllogism. We'll dissect a classic example to see the parts clearly.

Guided Path
1
Locate the proposition in the example
The author gives the proposition 'Every body is composed' as part of a syllogism. Identify its subject and predicate. The subject is 'body', the predicate is 'composed'.
The proposition is categorical: a statement about all members of a class.
2
Extract the terms by removing quantifier and copula
The author states that after analysis, only the subject and predicate remain as essential parts. Remove the quantifier 'every' and the copula 'is'. What remains are the two terms: 'body' and 'composed'.
The quantifier and copula are not considered terms because they are not part of the content but rather indicate quantity and connection.
3
Understand why modality is not a term
The author notes that modality (e.g., possibility, necessity) is also not among the essential parts of a proposition. Like the quantifier, it modifies the proposition but is not a term.
For example, in 'Necessarily, A is B', the word 'necessarily' is not a term; the terms are A and B.
4
Extend to the syllogism
In the full syllogism ('Every body is composed; every composed thing is temporally originated; therefore every body is temporally originated'), the three terms are 'body', 'composed', and 'temporally originated'. The middle term (composed) links the two premises.
This lays the foundation for understanding syllogistic structure and validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أجزاء المقدّمة وهي التي تبقى بعد التحليل فإنَّها تسمى حدودًا. فالمقدمة إذا كانت حمليّة تبقى بعد التحليل إلى آخر أجزائها الذاتية الموضوع والمحمول ليس إلا، إذ السُّور والجهة ليستا من الأجزاء الذاتية لها، والرابطة وإن كانت ذاتيَّة لكنَّها تدلُّ على الارتباط ولا مجال للارتباط بعد التحليل. ولنمثل للقياس والمقدّمة والحدود مثالاً وهو كلُّ جسم مؤلف، وكلُّ مؤلف محدث فكل جسم محدث فقولنا كل جسم مؤلَّف مقدِّمة وكذلك كلُّ مؤلَّف محدث مقدّمة أخرى. وأجزاؤهما من الجسم والمؤلف والمحدث حدود، ومجموع المقدمتين على النظم الذي مرَّ ذكره قياس، واللازم عنه وهو أنَّ كل محدث فذلك عند اللزوم نتيجة وقبل اللُّزوم عند استعمال الذهن بترتيب القياس وإقامته عليه مطلوب.
As for the parts of the proposition which remain after analysis, they are called terms. If the proposition is categorical, after analysis nothing remains except its essential parts, the subject and the predicate, because the quantifier and modality are not among its essential parts, and the copula, though essential, indicates connection, and there is no room for connection after analysis. Let us give an example of syllogism, proposition, and terms: 'Every body is composed, and every composed thing is temporally originated, so every body is temporally originated.' Our statement 'Every body is composed' is a proposition, and likewise 'Every composed thing is temporally originated' is another proposition. Their parts — body, composed, and temporally originated — are terms. The combination of the two premises according to the order mentioned is the syllogism, and the necessary consequent from it, which is 'every body is temporally originated', is, at the time of entailment, the conclusion, and before entailment, when the mind uses the ordering of the syllogism and establishes it, it is the desired.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage defines the parts of a proposition and gives an example. It opens by stating that the parts remaining after analysis are called hudud (terms). For a categorical proposition, the analysis leaves only subject and predicate; the quantifier (سور) and modality (جهة) are not essential parts, and the copula (رابطة), though essential, indicates relation which becomes irrelevant after decomposition. Then an example is given: the syllogism 'Every body is composed, and everything composed is temporally originated, therefore every body is temporally originated.' The two statements are premises (muqaddimat), and the terms are 'body', 'composed', and 'temporally originated'. The combination of the two premises in the correct order is the syllogism (qiyas). The necessary consequent (اللازم) is the conclusion (natija) after entailment, but before that it is termed the desired (matlub). This illustrates the basic structure of Aristotelian logic: premises → syllogism → conclusion.

Key Terms
حدّ
مقدّمة
قياس
نتيجة
مطلوب

Key Takeaway
The essential parts of a categorical proposition are its subject and predicate terms; quantifiers and modality are not part of the terms.

Definition of Wisdom as a Theoretical Craft
Author defines al-ḥikmah as a theoretical craft aiming for eternal happiness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in the definition of al-ḥikmah and explain its ultimate purpose as presented by the author.

Core Idea
Wisdom is a theoretical craft that builds a rational world mirroring the sensible one, aiming for eternal happiness.

In this passage, the author defines wisdom (al-ḥikmah) as a theoretical craft that enables the seeker to construct a rational world corresponding to the sensible world. This definition is not merely descriptive; it establishes wisdom as a discipline whose ultimate goal is eternal happiness. Let's break down its components to understand how the author structures this definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is 'al-ḥikmah' (wisdom). Locate it in the Arabic text: it is the subject of the sentence.
2
Extract the genus
The author places wisdom under the category 'ṣināʿah naẓariyyah' (theoretical craft). This genus indicates that wisdom is a discipline concerned with knowledge for its own sake, not practical action.
Contrast with practical crafts like agriculture or medicine.
3
Identify the differentia
The differentia is that wisdom enables one to 'complete a rational world corresponding to the sensible world.' This is what distinguishes wisdom from other theoretical crafts.
The rational world (ʿālam maʿqūl) is an intellectual representation of the physical universe.
4
Note the purpose beyond the definition
The author adds that through this, one becomes prepared for eternal happiness (al-saʿādah al-abadīyah) according to one's deeds and within human capacity. This teleological element gives the definition its Islamic character.
Is this part of the differentia or an additional property? Reflect on this.
5
Assess definition type
Determine whether this is a complete real definition (hadd ḥaqīqī) or a description (rasm). Since it includes genus and differentia, it seems a hadd, but the purpose clause may be added for completeness.
Compare with definitions of other sciences in the Islamic tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: [في الحكمة] واعلم بأن الحكمة صناعة نظرية؛ يكتسب منها حصول ما يجب عليه أن يكتسبه ليستكمل بذلك عالما معقولا مضاهيّا للعالم المحسوس. فيستعد للسعادة الأبدية على حسب ما يستحقه بكسبه؛ وذلك بقدر الطاقة البشرية، وحصول ما يفتقر إليه من الأسباب.
Chapter on Wisdom: Know that wisdom is a theoretical craft, from which is acquired the attainment of what one must acquire in order to thereby complete a rational world corresponding to the sensible world. Thus one becomes prepared for eternal happiness according to what one deserves by his earning. And that is to the extent of human capacity and attaining what one needs from the means.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage defines wisdom (al-ḥikmah) as a theoretical craft (ṣināʿah naẓariyyah). Its purpose is to acquire what one must acquire in order to complete a rational world (ʿālam maʿqūl) that corresponds to the sensible world (al-ʿālam al-mahsūs). Through this, one becomes prepared for eternal happiness according to one's deeds, within human capacity and by utilizing necessary means.

Key Terms
حكمة
صناعة نظرية
عالم معقول
عالم محسوس
سعادة أبدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Wisdom, defined as a theoretical craft that builds a rational world mirroring the sensible world, prepares the soul for eternal happiness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Defining Wisdom: Genus, Purpose, and Immutability
Defines wisdom as a theoretical craft and explains its goal of attaining eternal happiness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia of wisdom as defined in this passage, and explain the significance of its immutability for Islamic philosophy.

Core Idea
Wisdom's definition includes a genus (theoretical craft) and a purpose (attaining eternal happiness through knowledge of realities).

In this passage, the author presents a formal definition of wisdom (al-hikma). He classifies it as a theoretical craft and explains its ultimate goal: to prepare for eternal happiness by acquiring an intelligible world that mirrors the sensible world. Understanding this definition is crucial for appreciating how Islamic philosophers viewed the relationship between knowledge, reality, and salvation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus of wisdom
Find where the author places wisdom in a broader category. The genus is 'sina'a nazariyya' (theoretical craft). This distinguishes it from practical crafts.
The genus tells us the general class to which wisdom belongs.
2
Identify the differentia of wisdom
What distinguishes wisdom from other theoretical crafts? The differentia is the acquisition of an intelligible world (alim ma'qul) that corresponds to the sensible world, which prepares one for eternal happiness.
The differentia specifies the unique function of wisdom.
3
Determine the definition type
Is this a complete definition (hadd haqiqi) or a description (rasm)? The author gives the genus and a distinctive property (the function of leading to eternal happiness through knowledge). This qualifies as a hadd haqiqi because it captures the essence.
A complete definition includes both genus and differentia.
4
Analyze the claim of immutability
The author asserts that the truths of wisdom do not change with time or religion. This establishes wisdom as universal and eternal, not subject to historical or religious variation.
This claim underlines the objective and transcendent nature of philosophical knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: [في الحكمة ]
واعلم بأن الحكمة صناعة نظرية؛ يكتسب منها حصول ما يجب عليه أن يكتسبه ليستكمل بذلك عالما معقولا مضاهيّا للعالم المحسوس. فيستعد للسعادة الأبدية على حسب ما يستحقه بكسبه؛ وذلك بقدر الطاقة البشرية، وحصول ما يفتقر إليه من الأسباب. ولا يستراب في أن الغرض من الحكمة أن يوقف على حقائق الأشياء بحسب ما يمكنه الوقوف عليه. فإن الاستعداد للسعادة كما هو حقه لا يحصل إلا بالاطلاع على الحقائق وهو الاطلاع على العلوم الحكمية بالحقيقة. وما يكون منها فذلك لا يمكن أن يتغير بتغير الأزمان ولا أن يتبدل بتبدل الأديان كذلك بل يكون كما كان من الأزل إلى الأبد.
Section: On Wisdom. Know that wisdom is a theoretical craft; by it one acquires what he must acquire in order to complete thereby an intelligible world corresponding to the sensible world. He thereby prepares for eternal happiness according to what he deserves by his acquisition; that is to the extent of human capacity and the attainment of the causes he needs. And there is no doubt that the purpose of wisdom is to be informed of the realities of things to the extent possible for him to be informed. For preparation for happiness as is its right is only attained by insight into realities, and that is insight into the sciences of wisdom in reality. And what is of it cannot change with changing times nor change with changing religions, but rather it remains as it was from eternity to eternity.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of wisdom (al-hikma). The author begins a new section titled 'On Wisdom' and then defines wisdom as a theoretical craft (sina'a nazariyya). The purpose of this craft is to acquire what is necessary to complete an intelligible world (alim ma'qul) that corresponds to the sensible world (al-'alam al-mahsus). Through this, a person prepares for eternal happiness (al-sa'ada al-abadiyya) according to his efforts, within human capacity. The author then clarifies the ultimate goal (gharad) of wisdom: to understand the realities of things (haqa'iq al-ashya) as much as possible. This insight into realities is essential for achieving happiness. Notably, the author emphasizes that the truths of wisdom are immutable — they do not change with time or religion (adyan); they are eternal, from pre-eternity to post-eternity. This underscores the objective and universal nature of philosophical knowledge.

Classifying the Sciences: Theoretical and Practical
Author classifies theoretical and practical sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize any given science as theoretical or practical and identify its specific subdivision within the threefold classification.

Core Idea
Sciences are divided by their aim: theoretical sciences seek truth, practical sciences seek good.

This passage provides a foundational classification of the sciences in the Islamic intellectual tradition. The author distinguishes between theoretical sciences, which seek knowledge for its own sake, and practical sciences, which guide human action. Understanding this division is crucial for locating disciplines such as kalam, fiqh, and tasawwuf within the broader map of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish theoretical vs practical sciences
Look at the goal: theoretical sciences aim at knowledge of truth (haqq), practical sciences aim at knowledge of good (khayr).
2
Identify the three theoretical sciences
The author lists: natural (tabi'i), mathematical (riyadi), and divine (ilahi). These correspond to physics, mathematics, and metaphysics.
These are the traditional branches of theoretical philosophy.
3
Identify the three practical sciences
The author lists: ethics (akhlaq), household management (tadbir al-manzil), and city management (tadbir al-madinah). They are also called political sciences.
Note that 'political sciences' here includes all three, not just city management.
4
Examine the basis for the practical division
The division is by scope of human relations: individual alone, individual with household, individual with the general public.
This shows how Islamic thought integrates personal, familial, and societal domains.
5
Reflect on the author's use of the term 'political sciences'
The author broadens the term to include ethics and household management, but notes that common usage restricts it to city management.
This nuance is important when reading later works on siyasa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم كل واحد من الطبيعي والرياضي والإلهي فإنه قد يقوم مقام الأصل؛ وذلك على أقسام، وقد يقوم مقام الفرع؛ وذلك على أقسام كذلك يعرف من الكتب المخصوصة ببيانها.
وأما العلوم العملية فإنها تنقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام أيضًا؛ وذلك لأن ما يكون منها فذلك إما أن يكون متعلقا بآراء تنظم بها الأحوال المخصوصة بالإنسان وحده من الفضائل والرذائل؛ وهو علم الأخلاق. أو متعلقا بآراء تنظم بها الأحوال المشتركة بينه وبين خواصه؛ وهو علم تدبير المنزل. أو متعلقا بآراء تنظم بها الأحوال المشتركة بينه وبين عامة الناس؛ وهو علم تدبير المدينة. فهذه هي العلوم السياسية: الأول للنفس، والثاني لأهل المنزل، والثالث لأهل المدينة؛ على أن المشهور من علم السياسة والمفهوم منه هو الثالث منها.
ثم الغاية في الحكمة النظرية كما مرَّ ذكرها من قبل - معرفة الحق، وفي العملية معرفة الخير. ومن يؤتى الاستكمال بها من الحكمتين فقد أوتي خيرًا كثيرًا؛ وذلِكَ فَضْلُ اللَّهِ يُؤْتِيهِ مَن يَشَاءُ وَاللَّهُ ذُو الْفَضْلِ الْعَظِيمِ.
Then each of the natural, mathematical, and divine [sciences] may serve as a principle; that is in sections, and may serve as a branch; that is also in sections, as known from the books specifically explaining them.
As for the practical sciences, they are also divided into three sections; because that which is from them is either related to opinions that organize the conditions specific to man alone, from virtues and vices; and that is the science of ethics. Or related to opinions that organize the conditions shared between him and his household; and that is the science of household management. Or related to opinions that organize the conditions shared between him and the common people; and that is the science of city management. So these are the political sciences: the first for the soul, the second for the people of the household, and the third for the people of the city; although the well-known from the science of politics and what is understood from it is the third of them.
Then the goal in theoretical wisdom, as previously mentioned, is knowledge of truth, and in practical [wisdom] is knowledge of good. And whoever is given perfection through the two wisdoms has been given abundant good; 'That is the bounty of Allah which He gives to whom He wills, and Allah is the possessor of great bounty.' (Quran 57:21)
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage classifies the sciences. The author first notes that the three theoretical sciences (natural, mathematical, divine) can each serve either as a principle or as a branch. This distinction relates to their role in demonstration. Then he turns to the practical sciences, which are also three: ethics (concerned with individual virtues and vices), household management (concerned with the family), and city management (politics). He states that these are the 'political sciences,' with the first for the individual soul, the second for the household, and the third for the city. However, he notes that the term 'political science' commonly refers only to the third. Finally, he reiterates that the aim of theoretical wisdom is knowledge of truth, and of practical wisdom is knowledge of good, quoting Quran 57:21. This classification is foundational in Islamic philosophy; for example, theoretical sciences include kalam (theology) and physics, while practical sciences include fiqh (jurisprudence) and akhlaq (ethics).

Real vs Lexical Definitions: The Case of Knowledge
Author distinguishes between defining the essence and defining the meaning of the word.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a real definition (hadd haqiqi) and a lexical definition (hadd lafzi) and identify which kind a given definition is.

Core Idea
Not every definition aims at the essence; some merely clarify the word.

This passage presents an objection: there are many definitions of knowledge. The author first dismisses them as invalid for defining the essence, but then concedes that if taken as lexical definitions, some may be acceptable. This distinction is foundational in Islamic logic and crucial for rigorous thinking.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector lists several definitions of knowledge: clarifying a thing as it is, knowing a thing as it is, and what makes the subject knowledgeable. These are common in the tradition.
2
Separate the two levels of definition
The author distinguishes between 'definition of the meaning of the word' (ta'rīf ma'nā al-lafẓ) and 'definition of the essence of the thing' (ta'rīf māhiyyat al-'ilm). Understand the difference: one is lexical, the other is real.
Real definitions capture the quiddity (mahiyya); lexical definitions explain conventional usage.
3
Apply the distinction to the examples
Which of the given definitions would be acceptable as lexical definitions? Which would fail as real definitions? The author says some may be valid as lexical, but none valid as real.
4
Recognize the logical move
The author does not reject all definitions outright; he shifts the criterion. This is a common move in Islamic logic: clarifying the intention of a definition resolves apparent contradictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولئن قيل : كيف هو وقد كان من التفات من يعرف العلم بأنه عبارة عن تبيين الشيء كما هو هو، ومنهم من يعرفه بأنه عبارة عن معرفة الشيء على ما هو هو، ومنهم من يعرفه بأنه عبارة عما يوجب كون المحل عالمًا بالشيء، وعلى هذا فإن فيه من التعريفات المختلفة، فنقول : هذه التعريفات وما هو بمثلها فيما نحن فيه باطل . اللهم إلا أن يقال : المقصود من هذه التعريفات تعريف معنى اللفظ لا تعريف ماهية العلم؛ وحينئذ يصحُ البعض من هذه التعريفات لا كلها.
And if it is said: how is that, and there are those who know knowledge as an expression for clarifying a thing as it is, and some define it as an expression for knowing a thing as it is, and some define it as an expression for what makes the subject knowledgeable of a thing, and accordingly there are various definitions, so we say: these definitions and what is like them in our discussion are invalid. Unless it is said: the purpose of these definitions is to define the meaning of the word, not to define the essence of knowledge; then some of these definitions are valid, not all.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection: there are many well-known definitions of knowledge. The author dismisses them as invalid for defining the essence. However, he concedes that if these are taken as lexical definitions (defining the meaning of the word 'ilm), then some may be acceptable. This distinction is crucial in Islamic logic between defining the essence (hadd haqiqi) and defining the term (hadd lafzi). The author emphasizes that real definitions get at the essence, while lexical definitions merely explain common usage.

تعريف ماهية العلم

The same phrase can be a valid lexical definition but an invalid real definition; always check the intention.

The Three Types of Indication: Definitions and Mutual Relations
Author defines and explains three types of indication and their relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish mutabaqah, tadammun, and iltizam based on their definitions and mutual participations, and apply them to Islamic concepts.

Core Idea
Indication is not monolithic; the three types share core features but differ in what part of meaning is signified.

This passage from a classical logic text introduces the threefold division of indication (dalalah), a foundational tool for analyzing definitions and arguments in Islamic sciences. The author not only defines each type but also shows how they relate to each other through commonalities and differences. Understanding these relations helps you avoid confusion when a single word carries multiple indications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types and their definitions
Read the passage carefully and extract the definitions: mutabaqah (word indicates the entire meaning), tadammun (word indicates a part of the meaning), iltizam (word indicates a necessary external concomitant). Note the examples: 'house' for tadammun implies 'roof'; 'Creator' for iltizam implies 'existence of creation'.
These are not simply three separate categories; they are interrelated.
2
Analyze the triple commonality
The author states that all three types share (a) indicating a thing by a word, and (b) the signified being a single conventional meaning. Verify this from the text: 'each one of these three shares with the other in the indication of a thing upon a thing'.
This commonality makes them all valid types of dalalah.
3
Analyze the double commonalities
Now examine the pairwise sharings: (1) Mutabaqah and tadammun both do NOT indicate anything external to the meaning. (2) Mutabaqah and iltizam both do NOT indicate anything internal. (3) Tadammun and iltizam both do NOT indicate the meaning itself. The author uses these to show how each type differs while still being under the same genus.
This analysis prepares you to identify which type is at work in a given expression.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the word 'sawm' (fasting). Mortabaqah: indicates the act of abstaining from certain things. Tadammun: indicates the intention or the time of fasting. Iltizam: indicates the reward or the obligation. Identify which type is appropriate for a given legal discussion.
In Usul al-Fiqh, these distinctions determine how far a ruling can extend.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في الدلالة وأقسامها ]
واعلم بأن الدلالة بل هي أو غيرها أي شيء كان لا يمكن أن يكون منقسما إلى ثلاثة أقسام مثلا دفعة واحدة، بل يكون منقسما إلى القسمين أولا، ثم ينقسم إلى القسمين ثانيا؛ والأقسام الثلاثة مشتملة على القسمة مرتين والأربعة كذلك ثلاث مرات، وعلى هذا في الخمسة وغيرها. وإنها أي الأقسام إذا كانت وجودية فلا تكون القسمة منحصرة فيها إلا وأن يكون البعض منها عبارة عن عدم ما عداه في الوجود، والأقسام من بعد لا تكون خالية عن المشاركات والمباينات والمناسبات كما في الأقسام الثلاثة التي نحن بصددها، فإن فيها من المشاركات / ثلاثية تارة وثنائية أخرى. أما الثلاثية فإن كل واحد من هذه الثلاثة يشارك غيره في دلالة الشيء على الشيء، وفي دلالة اللفظ على الشيء كذلك، وإن ذلك الشيء أي المدلول عليه (1) مفرد بالوضع في كل واحد منها.
(1) النسخة- أي المدلول عليه صح هامش.
وأما الثنائية فالأول منها وهو الذي يكون بطريق المطابقة يشارك الثاني في أن اللفظ في كل واحد منهما لا يدل على ما يكون خارجًا عن مسمى اللفظ لازما كان ذلك الخارج أو غير لازم بينا كان أو غير بين. ويشارك الأول (2) الثالث في أن اللفظ في كل واحد منهما لا يدل على ما يكون داخلا في المسمى عاما كان ذلك الداخل أو خاصا، قريباً كان أو بعيدًا. وكذلك الثاني يشارك الثالث في أن اللفظ في كل واحد منهما لا يدل على المسمى أصلًا.
وعلى هذا في المناسبات فإنها قد تكون ثلاثية وقد كان اللفظ في كل واحد منها يدل على شيء لا يدل على ذلك الشيء غيره، وقد تكون ثنائية كذلك. والأول منها لا يشارك الثاني في شيء إلا ويباين الثالث في ذلك الشيء، وكذلك في الثاني منها والثالث. وأما المناسبات فقد مر ذكرها من قبل، لما مر ذكر اللازمية والملزومية بينها. وهذه الأمور كلها من جملة ما لا يشتبه على العاقل بعد الاطلاع على حقائق تلك الأقسام.
ذلك
ثم الأول منها يسمى بالمطابقة لانطباق كل فرد من أفراد هذا اللفظ على كل فرد من أفراد هذا المعنى، فلا يمكن أن يتجاوز هذا اللفظ عن المسمى في هذه الدلالة البتة؛ والثاني يسمى بالتضمن؛ لما أن المسمى متضمن للمدلول عليه في هذه الدلالة؛ والثالث بالالتزام لما أن المدلول عليه لازم من لوازم المسمى على وجه ينتقل الذهن من المسمى إليه. فكما أن اللفظ يدل على المسمى على التفسير الذي مرَّ ذكره فكذلك المسمى على ما يكون من اللوازم في قسمي الثاني منها والثالث.
وقد قيل في ترجيح البعض منها على البعض ؛ وذلك أن دلالة المطابقة أقوى من دلالة التضمن لما أنها أصل لما عداها ، ودلالة التضمن أقوى من
Section on Indication and Its Divisions. Know that indication, whether it is or something else, any thing cannot be divided into three divisions, for example, all at once. Rather, it is divided into two divisions first, then divided into two divisions second. The three divisions involve division twice, and the four divisions involve division three times, and so on for five and others. And if the divisions are existential, then the division is not restricted to them unless some of them are expressions of the absence of what is other than them in existence. Afterwards, the divisions are not free from commonalities, differences, and correspondences, as in the three divisions we are dealing with. Indeed, among them are commonalities: sometimes triple and sometimes double. As for the triple: each one of these three shares with the other in the indication of a thing upon a thing, and in the indication of a word upon a thing likewise, and that thing, i.e., the signified, is individually according to the convention in each one of them. As for the double: the first, which is by way of correspondence, shares with the second in that the word in each of them does not indicate what is external to the meaning of the word, whether that external is a necessary concomitant or not, whether evident or not. And the first shares with the third in that the word in each of them does not indicate what is internal to the meaning, whether that internal is general or specific, near or far. Likewise, the second shares with the third in that the word in each of them does not indicate the meaning at all. And similarly for correspondences: they are sometimes triple and the word in each of them indicates a thing that no other indicates, and sometimes double likewise. The first does not share with the second in anything except that it differs from the third in that thing, and similarly for the second and third. As for correspondences, their mention has already passed, due to the mention of necessary concomitance and being necessary concomitant among them. All these matters are among what are not ambiguous to the intelligent after having knowledge of the realities of those divisions. Then the first is called correspondence (mutabaqah) because of the conformity of every individual of this word to every individual of this meaning, so it is impossible for this word to go beyond the meaning in this indication at all. The second is called implication (tadammun) because the meaning contains the signified in this indication. The third is called entailment (iltizam) because the signified is a necessary concomitant of the meaning in a way that the mind transfers from the meaning to it. Just as the word indicates the meaning according to the interpretation mentioned, likewise the meaning indicates what are its necessary concomitants in the second and third divisions. It has been said concerning the preference of some of them over others: that the indication of correspondence is stronger than the indication of implication because it is the basis of the others, and the indication of implication is stronger than...
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory passage from a work on logic. The author begins a new section on indication (dalalah) and its divisions. First, he makes a methodological point: a division into three parts does not happen all at once; rather, it is done by first dividing into two, then subdividing. Thus, the threefold division actually results from two successive binary divisions. The same applies to fourfold (three divisions) and so on. He also notes that if the divisions are existential (i.e., actual categories), one of them may be merely the absence of the others. Then the author focuses on the three divisions at hand: correspondence (mutabaqah), implication (tadammun), and entailment (iltizam). He explains the commonalities (musharakat) among them: a triple commonality—each shares with the others in being an indication of a thing by a word, and each shares in that the signified is a single conventional meaning. Then he details the double commonalities: the first (mutabaqah) shares with the second (tadammun) in that neither indicates anything outside the meaning of the word (i.e., no extraneous element). The first shares with the third (iltizam) in that neither indicates anything internal to the meaning. The second shares with the third in that neither indicates the meaning itself at all. The author also discusses correspondences (munasabat) in a similar manner. Finally, he provides the definitions: mutabaqah is so called because the word corresponds exactly to the meaning, without exceeding it. Tadammun is because the meaning contains the signified (e.g., 'house' implies 'roof'). Iltizam is because the signified is a necessary concomitant of the meaning, such that the mind transfers from the meaning to it. The passage ends with a note on the relative strength of these types, stating that mutabaqah is strongest because it is the basis, and tadammun is stronger than iltizam, though the sentence is incomplete.

Key Takeaway
Indication (dalalah) is divided into three types based on what the word signifies: the entire meaning (mutabaqah), a part (tadammun), or an external necessary concomitant (iltizam).

Defining the Three Types of Linguistic Indication
Author introduces the threefold division and the hierarchy within each type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define and differentiate between dalalat al-mutabaqa, dalalat al-tadammun, and dalalat al-iltizam, and identify which type is used in a given classical example.

Core Idea
A word can indicate its full meaning, a part of it, or a necessary external concomitant.

In this passage, the author begins by establishing a key principle: when a word indicates something, it can do so in three distinct ways. Understanding these three types is foundational for all subsequent discussions of definition and meaning in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Look at the key terms. Mutual indication (mutabaqa) is the word indicating its entire meaning; connotative (tadammun) indicates a part; implicative (iltizam) indicates something outside but necessarily connected.
The terms are stable across the tradition.
2
Distinguish near vs. distant
The author notes that indications of near parts/concomitants are stronger than distant ones. For example, 'human' indicates 'animal' (near part) more strongly than 'body' (distant part).
This hierarchy applies within each type.
3
Test with examples
Take the word 'wine' (khamr). Its mutual indication is the intoxicating drink; connotative indication is its liquid nature; implicative indication is its prohibition. Identify which is which.
Islamic examples ensure relevance.
4
Apply the hierarchy
For the word 'prayer' (salat), rank its parts (ruku', sujud) and its conditions (tahara). According to the passage, which indication is stronger, and why?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلالة الالتزام لما أن العلم نحو الماهية يجري مجرى العلم بالماهية. وعلى هذا دلالة اللفظ على الأجزاء القريبة أقوى من الدلالة على الأجزاء البعيدة، وعلى اللازم القريب أقوى من الدلالة على اللازم البعيد. وقد يقال فيه دلالة التضمن والالتزام من الدلالات العقلية، والدلالة العقلية أقوى من الدلالة الوضعية، وهي دلالة المطابقة لما أن العقلية لا تكون قابلة للتبدل والتغير. غير أنه في حيز المنع لما أنهما وإن كانتا من الدلالات العقلية فحصولهما يفتقر إلى حصول الوضعية، ولأن الترجيح لا يمكن إلا وأن يكون البعض منها في مقابلة البعض، وعند ذلك تكون الوضعية أقوى من العقلية فيما نحن فيه.
The implicative indication is because knowledge about the quiddity runs in the same course as knowledge of the quiddity. On this basis, a word's indication of near parts is stronger than its indication of distant parts, and its indication of a near necessary concomitant is stronger than its indication of a distant necessary concomitant. It is said therein that connotative and implicative indication are among intellectual indications, and intellectual indication is stronger than conventional indication—which is the mutual indication—because the intellectual is not susceptible to change and alteration. However, this is subject to objection because, even if they are intellectual indications, their actualization depends on the actualization of the conventional indication, and because comparison is only possible when one is set against the other, and in that case the conventional indication is stronger than the intellectual in this context.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage defines and compares the three types of linguistic indication: mutual (dalālat al-muṭābaqa), connotative (dalālat al-taḍammun), and implicative (dalālat al-iltizām). The author begins by specifying that implicative indication operates through knowledge of the quiddity (māhiyya), arguing that the word's indication of close parts or necessary concomitants is stronger than that of distant ones. This establishes an ordering of strength within each type. The passage then presents a claim: connotative and implicative indications are intellectual (ʿaqliyya), and intellectual indications are stronger than conventional (waḍʿiyya) ones (i.e., mutual indication), because intellectual meanings are fixed and not subject to change. However, the author immediately rejects this claim (manʿ), giving two reasons: first, even if they are intellectual, their realization depends on the prior existence of the conventional indication (the word must first signify the whole by convention); second, when comparing strength, only when both types are put on the same level can a judgment be made, and in this context conventional indication is actually stronger. This shows a nuanced position: while intellectual indications are more stable in principle, they are weaker in terms of priority and independence. The argument is dialectical, presenting a claim and refuting it.

Key Terms
دلالة المطابقة (dalālat al-muṭābaqa): The indication of a word that corresponds exactly to its full meaning (e.g., 'human' indicating the whole animal rationale). [Note: Stable term across the tradition.]
دلالة التضمن (dalālat al-taḍammun): The indication of a word for a part of its meaning (e.g., 'human' indicating 'animal'). Here called an intellectual indication. [Note: Standard definition.]
دلالة الالتزام (dalālat al-iltizām): The indication of a word for something external to its meaning but necessarily concomitant (e.g., 'human' indicating 'capable of laughter'). [Note: Standard definition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of connotative indication (dalalat al-tadammun)?
A) The word 'human' indicating the whole species.
B) The word 'human' indicating the property 'animal'.
C) The word 'human' indicating the capacity for laughter.
D) The word 'human' indicating the word itself.

Key Takeaway
Words indicate their meanings in three distinct ways: fully, partially, or through necessary concomitants, with a hierarchy of strength based on proximity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the word 'zakat' in the Quran. Identify which type of indication is used for 'giving a portion of wealth' (the full meaning), for 'the act of giving' (a part), and for 'purification of the soul' (a necessary concomitant).
?
For the word 'fasting' (sawm), list an example of each type of indication from Islamic rulings.

The Strength of Intellectual vs. Conventional Indication
Author debates the relative strength of intellectual vs conventional indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the argument that conventional indication is stronger than intellectual indication in the context of linguistic meaning, and identify the logical structure used.

Core Idea
Conventional indication is prior and stronger because intellectual indications depend on it for their activation.

The author presents a common view that intellectual indications (tadammun and iltizam) are stronger due to their immutability, then immediately refutes it with a precise logical objection. This debate reveals an important principle: the strength of an indication depends on its independence and priority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being attacked
The claim: 'Intellectual indication (tadammun and iltizam) is stronger than conventional indication (mutabaqa) because it is not subject to change.'
2
Locate the objection (man')
The author says 'غير أنه في حيز المنع' (this is subject to objection). The objection gives two reasons: (a) intellectual indications depend on conventional indication for their actualization; (b) comparison requires setting one against the other, and when done, conventional is stronger.
The objection is a valid challenge.
3
Analyze the logical structure
The argument is a modus tollens: If intellectual were stronger, it would be independent. But it is dependent. Therefore it is not stronger; conventional is stronger.
Formalize to see the validity.
4
Assess the strength of the objection
Does the objection successfully refute the claim? The author uses 'غير أنه في حيز المنع' indicating a valid challenge; he does not provide a counter-response, so the objection stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The implicative indication is because knowledge about the quiddity runs in the same course as knowledge of the quiddity. On this basis, a word's indication of near parts is stronger than its indication of distant parts, and its indication of a near necessary concomitant is stronger than its indication of a distant necessary concomitant. It is said therein that connotative and implicative indication are among intellectual indications, and intellectual indication is stronger than conventional indication—which is the mutual indication—because the intellectual is not susceptible to change and alteration. However, this is subject to objection because, even if they are intellectual indications, their actualization depends on the actualization of the conventional indication, and because comparison is only possible when one is set against the other, and in that case the conventional indication is stronger than the intellectual in this context.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage defines and compares the three types of linguistic indication: mutual (dalālat al-muṭābaqa), connotative (dalālat al-taḍammun), and implicative (dalālat al-iltizām). The author begins by specifying that implicative indication operates through knowledge of the quiddity (māhiyya), arguing that the word's indication of close parts or necessary concomitants is stronger than that of distant ones. This establishes an ordering of strength within each type. The passage then presents a claim: connotative and implicative indications are intellectual (ʿaqliyya), and intellectual indications are stronger than conventional (waḍʿiyya) ones (i.e., mutual indication), because intellectual meanings are fixed and not subject to change. However, the author immediately rejects this claim (manʿ), giving two reasons: first, even if they are intellectual, their realization depends on the prior existence of the conventional indication (the word must first signify the whole by convention); second, when comparing strength, only when both types are put on the same level can a judgment be made, and in this context conventional indication is actually stronger. This shows a nuanced position: while intellectual indications are more stable in principle, they are weaker in terms of priority and independence. The argument is dialectical, presenting a claim and refuting it.

Key Terms
دلالة المطابقة (dalālat al-muṭābaqa): The indication of a word that corresponds exactly to its full meaning (e.g., 'human' indicating the whole animal rationale). [Note: Stable term across the tradition.]
دلالة التضمن (dalālat al-taḍammun): The indication of a word for a part of its meaning (e.g., 'human' indicating 'animal'). Here called an intellectual indication. [Note: Standard definition.]
دلالة الالتزام (dalālat al-iltizām): The indication of a word for something external to its meaning but necessarily concomitant (e.g., 'human' indicating 'capable of laughter'). [Note: Standard definition.]
دلالة عقلية (dalāla ʿaqliyya): Indication that relies on intellectual inference, not conventional assignment. Here claimed to be stronger due to immutability, but refuted. [Note: In later texts, often contrasted with waḍʿiyya; here the author argues it is weaker in one respect.]
دلالة وضعية (dalāla waḍʿiyya): Indication based on conventional assignment of a word to a meaning. Here said to be stronger than ʿaqliyya in the context of dependence. [Note: Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the conventional indication (mutabaqa) ultimately stronger than intellectual indications (tadammun and iltizam)?
A) Because intellectual indications are subject to change.
B) Because conventional indication is innate (fiṭrī).
C) Because intellectual indications depend on the prior existence of conventional indication.
D) Because conventional indication conveys more information.

Key Takeaway
Intellectual indications are not independent; they rely on conventional assignment, making conventional indication stronger in linguistic analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Levels of Conventional and Intellectual Indication
Author discusses levels of conventional and intellectual indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish conventional indication from intellectual indication (taḍammun and iltizām) and identify the different types of necessary concomitants (lāzim dhihnī, lāzim khārijī, bayyin, ghayr bayyin).

Core Idea
Conventional indication has one level; intellectual indications can have multiple levels of meaning.

This lesson delves into the levels of indication as discussed in classical logic. You will learn why conventional indication (muṭābaqa) is limited to a single level due to its basis in positing, while intellectual indications (taḍammun and iltizām) can extend to multiple layers. We will also examine how necessary concomitants are classified and why implicative indication is less commonly used in the sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the single level of conventional indication
Find the statement that conventional indication is only on one level. Understand that this is because it depends on an act of positing (waḍʿ) and therefore does not exceed it. Contrast this with intellectual indications.
The passage begins with 'ثم الوضعية لا تكون إلا على مرتبة واحدة'.
2
Explore the multiple levels in taḍammun and iltizām
Notice that in connotative indication (taḍammun), a word can indicate a part and then a part of that part (e.g., 'human' → 'animal' → 'sentient moving by will'). Similarly, in implicative indication (iltizām), it can indicate a necessary concomitant and then a necessary concomitant of that concomitant (e.g., 'animal' → 'potentially moving' → 'potentially occupying space').
Examples in the passage use 'human' and 'animal'.
3
Analyze the objection about infinite regress in iltizām
The author addresses a potential problem: if every necessary concomitant had another necessary concomitant ad infinitum, that would be impossible. He argues that the chain stops because not every necessary concomitant entails another. For instance, oddness for three is a necessary concomitant but does not entail a further necessary concomitant.
The key phrase: 'وإلا لكان لكل لازم في كل صورة لازم آخر لا إلى نهاية، وذلك محال'.
4
Classify necessary concomitants as mental/external and evident/non-evident
Distinguish between mental necessary concomitant (lāzim dhihnī) without which the quiddity cannot be conceived (e.g., oddness for three), and external necessary concomitant (lāzim khārijī) which exists externally (e.g., attraction for a body). Also note that some necessary concomitants are evident (bayyin) and instantly known, while others are not.
The passage states: 'اللازم فيما ذكرنا من الدلالات هو الذهني' and gives examples like 'oddness for three' as evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الوضعية لا تكون إلا على مرتبة واحدة لما أنها بحسب الوضع فلا يكون وجودها بدونه على خلاف العقلية في دلالتي التضمن والالتزام فإنه يمكن في التضمن أن يدل اللفظ على الجزء وعلى جزء ذلك الجزء، كما في لفظ الإنسان فإنه يدل على الحيوان وعلى جزئه وهو الحساس المتحرك بالإرادة كذلك. وكذلك في الالتزام، فإنه يمكن أن يدل على اللازم وعلى لازم ذلك اللازم أيضًا، كما في لفظ الحيوان فإنه يدل على المتحرك بالقوة وعلى لازمه وهو المتحيز بالقوة كذلك. والانتقال في دلالة التضمن أنها من جملة ما ليس بها مراتب وإلا لكان أول الأجزاء في التأليف آخرها في الدلالة. وحينئذ يلزم تأخير فهم الجزء عن فهم الكل، وذلك على خلاف الأصل فإنه لا يلزم أن يكون كذلك. وقد كان الذي يدل على المسمى من الألفاظ فإنه لا يدل على كل جزء من أجزائه، بل يدل على كل جزء ينتقل الذهن من المسمى إليه، ودلالة اللفظ على كل جزء من تلك الأجزاء على السواء فلا يتقدم البعض منها على البعض في الدلالة، وإن كان المسمى يتقدم على الكل في الدلالة بحسب الفهم). وكذلك لا يقال في دلالة الالتزام إن المراد من اللوازم إذا كانت ثبوتية فلا يلزم أن يكون الدال على المسمى من الألفاظ يدل على اللازم، وإلا لكان لكل لازم في كل صورة لازم آخر لا إلى نهاية، وذلك محال. فإنه لا يكون محالا وقد كان من الممتنع أن لا يكون لكل لازم لازم، إذا كان كل لازم يستلزم أحدهما وهو إما اللازم أو الملزوم مثلا بالضرورة، ولأنه لا يلزم من كون اللفظ ولا على الشيء لأن ما كان ذلك الشيء لغيره أو ملزوم له أن يكون ذلك الشيء ثابتا في نفس الأمر وهذا ظاهر ثم اللازم بالشيء قد يكون بينا يلزم من العلم بالشيء العلم بذلك اللازم كما هو وقد لا يكون نحو الحمى للأسد مثلا، وما يكون بينا فذلك قد يكون بينا يلزم من العلم بالشيء العلم به دفعة نحو الفردية للثلاثة وأمثالها، وقد لا يكون نحو الفردية مع كونها لازمة واللازم لا تتقدم العقلية لولا تتقدم الذهنية (٢) أيضًا قد يكون لازما بالنسبة إلى الوجود للخارجي نحو الجاذبة للجسم مثلا، ولا يلزم من كون الشيء لازما في الذهن أن يكون لازما في الخارج؛ لأنه لا يلزم من العلم بالجسم في الذهن العلم بالجاذبة (۳)، وكذلك لا يلزم من كون الشيء لازما في الخارج أن يكون لازما في الذهن. واللازم فيما ذكرنا من الدلالات هو الذهني لما أنها اللازم الذهني من قبل ما يكون بحسب العلم لا يمكن تصور الماهية بدونها (4) بحسب العلم، والمشهور في الثالثة منها وهي دلالة الالتزام أنها مهجورة الاستعمال في العلوم
Then the conventional indication is only on one level because it is according to positing, so its existence does not exist without it, unlike the intellectual in the two indications of connotation and implication. For in connotation it is possible for the word to indicate the part and the part of that part, as in the word 'human' for it indicates 'animal' and its part, which is 'sentient moving by will' likewise. And similarly in implication, it is possible for it to indicate the necessary concomitant and also the necessary concomitant of that necessary concomitant, as in the word 'animal' for it indicates 'potentially moving' and its necessary concomitant, which is 'potentially occupying space' likewise. And the transition in connotative indication is that it is among what has no levels; otherwise the first of the parts in composition would be the last in indication. Then the understanding of the part would be delayed after the understanding of the whole, contrary to the principle, for it is not necessary that it be so. And the word which indicates the named does not indicate every part of its parts; rather it indicates every part to which the mind moves from the named, and the indication of the word of each part of those parts is equally, so none of them precedes another in indication, even if the named precedes the whole in indication according to understanding. And likewise it is not said in implicative indication that the intended of the necessary concomitants—if they are positive—then it is not necessary that the indicator of the named from among words indicate the necessary concomitant; otherwise there would be for every necessary concomitant in every form another necessary concomitant ad infinitum, and that is impossible. For it is not impossible and it was among the impossible that every necessary concomitant does not have a necessary concomitant, if every necessary concomitant entails one of them—that is either the necessary concomitant or the entailed, for example—necessarily, and because it does not follow from the word being upon the thing—since that thing is for other than it or entailed by it—that that thing is established in actual fact, and this is apparent. Then the necessary concomitant of a thing may be evident such that knowledge of the thing entails knowledge of that necessary concomitant as it is, and it may not be like 'fever' for 'lion' for example. And what is evident, that may be evident such that knowledge of the thing entails knowledge of it at once like 'oddness' for 'three' and similar, and it may not be like 'oddness' even though it is necessary. And the necessary concomitant does not precede the intellectual were it not for the precedence of the mental. Also it may be a necessary concomitant with respect to external existence like 'attraction' for 'body' for example, and it does not follow from a thing being necessary in the mind that it is necessary in the external; because it does not follow from knowledge of the body in the mind knowledge of attraction. And likewise it does not follow from a thing being necessary in the external that it is necessary in the mind. And the necessary concomitant in what we mentioned of the indications is the mental, because it is the mental necessary concomitant of what is according to knowledge; the quiddity cannot be conceived without it according to knowledge. And the well-known regarding the third of them, which is implicative indication, is that it is abandoned in use in the sciences.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion by examining the levels of indication. The author first states that conventional indication (muṭābaqa) is limited to one level because it is based on a single act of positing (waḍʿ). In contrast, intellectual indications (taḍammun and iltizām) can have multiple levels: a word can indicate a part and then a part of that part (e.g., 'human' → 'animal' → 'sentient, moving by will'), and similarly a word can indicate a necessary concomitant and then a necessary concomitant of that concomitant (e.g., 'animal' → 'potentially moving' → 'potentially occupying space'). The author then addresses an objection regarding connotative indication: if parts had levels, the first part in composition would be last in indication, delaying understanding of the part until after the whole—contrary to the norm. Instead, the word indicates each part equally without priority. For implicative indication, the author refutes the worry of infinite regress: if every necessary concomitant had another necessary concomitant ad infinitum, that would be impossible. But it is not necessary that every necessary concomitant has another; the entailment relation stops at some point. The passage further distinguishes between evident (bayyin) and non-evident necessary concomitants, and between mental (dhihnī) and external (khārijī) necessary concomitants. A thing may be a necessary concomitant in the mind but not in external existence, and vice versa. The author concludes that the necessary concomitant relevant to the discussion here is the mental one, without which the quiddity cannot be conceived. Finally, it notes that implicative indication is often abandoned in scientific usage (likely because it is less reliable). The passage is dense with distinctions and dialectical moves.

Key Terms
اللازم الذهني
اللازم الخارجي
بين
التأليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it not necessary for implicative indication to have an infinite chain of necessary concomitants?
A) Because all necessary concomitants are evident and need no further explanation.
B) Because the chain stops at a necessary concomitant that does not itself entail another.
C) Because the word cannot indicate more than one necessary concomitant.
D) Because infinite regress is possible in the mind but not in external existence.

Key Takeaway
Conventional indication is single-level, while intellectual indications can have multiple levels; necessary concomitants are classified as mental/external and evident/non-evident, and implicative indication is often avoided in scientific discourse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Identify its conventional indication (the specific obligation), its connotative indication (e.g., giving a portion of wealth), and its implicative indication (e.g., the necessity of purifying one's wealth). Explain whether the implicative indication is mental or external.
?
