⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جزء من أجزاء معناه أصلا ولا على غيره كذلك. والمركب منها ما يكون دالا على شيء ما حين هو جزؤه كقولنا: الإنسان حيوان مثلا، ولا يستراب في أن جزءا ما منه، بل كل واحد من جزئيه يدل على شيء ما حين هو جزؤه. ثم المفرد على التفسير الذي مرَّ ذكره، فذلك من جملة ما ذهب إليه أرسطو ومن تابعه؛ وعن المتأخرين منهم، أنهم قالوا: «المفرد هو الذي لا يدل جزء منه على جزء من أجزاء معناه احترازًا عن قولنا : عبد الله إذا كان علمًا لكنه قد يذكر ويراد به العلم، وقد يذكر ويراد به النعت والصفة». والأول منهما هو اللفظ المفرد بالضرورة ولا دلالة لجزء من أجزائه البتة؛ إذ العلم قائم مقام الإشارة إلى ذلك الشيء المعين، ولهذا قالت النحاة: «الأسماء الأعلام لا تفيد فائدة في المسمى بل هي قائمة مقام الإشارات. والعلم قد يكون من الألفاظ المهملة وقد يكون من الألفاظ المستعملة، ولا تفاوت بينهما في العلمية، إذا كان العلم الشيء عبارة عما يعلم به ذلك الشيء. وأما الزيادة في تعريف المفرد وهو أن لا يدل جزء منه على جزء من أجزاء معناه على اعتقاد أن بعض أجزاء الألفاظ المفردة ولا يدل على شيء لا يكون ذلك الشيء جزءًا من أجزاء معناه نحو العبد في قولنا : عبد الله، إذا كان علمًا فإنه يدل على شيء وإن لم يكن ذلك الشيء جزءًا من أجزاء معنى الجملة فذلك خطأ لما أن اللفظ لا يدل على المعنى لذاته بل بالوضع والاصطلاح، فيكون دلالته على المعنى الذي يكون استعماله لتعريف ذلك المعنى بقصد المتلفظ وإرادته، ولا قصد للمتلفظ و لا للواضع كذلك أن يدل جزء المفرد على شيء أصلًا حين هو جزؤه، ولو كان كذلك فلا دلالة لجزئه البتة. والثاني هو المركب وذلك لا يكون واردا على ما قلناه وإنه – أي المركب- بالنسبة إلى المفرد على التفسير الأول هو الذي يدل على شيء ما حين هو جزؤه، وبالنسبة إليه على التفسير الثاني هو الذي يدل جزؤه على جزء من أجزاء معناه.
A part of its meaning not originally and not otherwise. The compound is what signifies something when it is a part of it, like our saying 'man is an animal', and there is no doubt that some part of it, rather each of its two parts signifies something when it is a part of it. Then the simple according to the interpretation previously mentioned is part of what Aristotle and his followers held; and from the later ones among them, they said: 'The simple is that of which no part signifies any part of its meaning, as a precaution against our saying 'Abdullah' when it is a proper noun, but it may be mentioned and intended as a proper noun, and may be mentioned and intended as an adjective and attribute.' The first of these two is necessarily a simple utterance and no part of its parts indicates at all, because the proper noun stands in place of pointing to that particular thing, and for this reason the grammarians said: 'Proper nouns do not convey a benefit about the named thing but stand in place of pointers.' And a proper noun may be from neglected utterances or from used utterances, and there is no difference between them in proper-noun-ness, if the proper noun of a thing is an expression for that by which that thing is known. As for the addition in the definition of the simple, that no part of it signifies any part of its meaning, on the belief that some parts of simple utterances do not signify anything that is not a part of its meaning, like 'abd' in our saying 'Abdullah' when it is a proper noun—for it signifies something even if that something is not part of the meaning of the whole—that is an error, because an utterance does not signify meaning by itself but by convention and usage, so its signification is of the meaning which its use is for defining that meaning by the intent and will of the speaker, and the speaker or the positer has no intent that a part of the simple should signify anything at all when it is a part of it; and if it were so, then there would be no signification of its part at all. The second is the compound, and that is not forthcoming according to what we said; and it—the compound—in relation to the simple according to the first interpretation is that which signifies something when it is a part of it, and in relation to it according to the second interpretation is that of which a part signifies a part of its meaning.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed definition of simple (mufrad) and compound (murakkab) utterances, drawing from Aristotle and later logicians. The author begins by stating that a compound is something that signifies when it is a part, giving the example 'man is an animal' where each part (man, animal) signifies something as a part. The simple, according to the earlier interpretation, is then defined. The author then presents the later logicians' definition: the simple is that of which no part signifies any part of its meaning, specifically to exclude cases like 'Abdullah' when it is a proper noun. The author explains that in a proper noun, the whole stands in place of a pointer, and the parts do not contribute to the meaning; hence it is simple. The author then critiques an additional condition some add to the definition of the simple—that no part signifies anything at all, even if that thing is not part of the meaning of the whole. Using the example of 'Abd' in 'Abdullah' as a proper noun, the author argues that this condition is erroneous because signification is by convention and the intent of the speaker; the positer did not intend the part to signify anything. Thus, the part's signification is irrelevant to the simplicity of the whole. Finally, the author reiterates the two interpretations of the compound: according to the first, it signifies when it is a part; according to the second, its part signifies a part of its meaning.

Key Terms
المفرد
المركب
العلم
الوضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a murakkab (compound) utterance according to the author?
A) 'Zayd'
B) 'Abdullah' when used as a proper noun
C) 'al-insanu hayawan'
D) 'al-Raḥmān' (the Most Merciful)

Key Takeaway
A simple utterance is defined by the fact that no part signifies part of its meaning; a compound utterance is defined by the opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following utterances as mufrad or murakkab, providing a brief justification: a) 'al-Raḥmān' (the Most Merciful), b) 'ṣalāt al-fajr' (dawn prayer), c) 'Muḥammad' (as a proper name).
?
Explain why 'Abdullah' when used as a proper noun is considered mufrad according to the definition.

Proper Nouns as Simple Utterances
Author defines mufrad and murakkab, cites later logicians, and refutes a mistaken condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why proper nouns are considered mufrad despite their parts having independent meanings.

Core Idea
Proper nouns are pointers; their whole meaning is not composed of their parts.

The passage discusses the special status of proper nouns (a‘lām) like 'Abdullah'. Although 'Abd' and 'Allah' each have meanings, when combined as a proper noun, the whole stands in place of a pointer to a specific individual. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind treating such names as simple utterances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the example 'Abdullah'
The author uses 'Abdullah' as a proper noun. In this usage, it refers to a specific person, not to 'servant of Allah' in a descriptive sense.
2
Distinguish the two possible usages
'Abdullah' can be used as a proper noun (علم) or as an adjective/attribute (نعت وصفة). The author says that when it is a proper noun, it is necessarily mufrad because the whole acts as a pointer.
If it were used as an attribute, it would be compound.
3
Understand the pointer function
The grammarians (النحاة) said: 'Proper nouns do not convey a benefit about the named thing but stand in place of pointers.' This means the name directly refers to the individual without describing them through the parts' meanings.
So the parts do not signify part of the meaning of the whole.
4
Apply the definition of mufrad
According to the definition, mufrad is that of which no part signifies part of its meaning. Since in a proper noun the parts are not intended to signify, the whole qualifies as mufrad.
The author emphasizes that the speaker or positer did not intend the parts to signify anything.

Refuting the Erroneous Condition on Simple Utterances
Author defines mufrad and murakkab, cites later logicians, and refutes a mistaken condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute the erroneous condition that no part of a simple utterance may signify anything at all.

Core Idea
Signification is by convention and speaker intent; a part's own signification does not affect simplicity.

The author critiques an additional condition some later logicians added to the definition of mufrad: that no part may signify anything at all, even if that thing is not part of the meaning of the whole. Using the example of 'Abd' in 'Abdullah', the author shows why this condition is mistaken. This lesson walks through his logical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the erroneous condition
Some added to the definition of mufrad the requirement that no part of it signifies anything at all, not even something outside the meaning of the whole. This is the condition the author rejects.
The condition goes beyond the original definition.
2
Examine the counterexample
The author uses 'Abd' in 'Abdullah' as a proper noun. 'Abd' can signify 'servant' even though that is not part of the meaning of 'Abdullah' as a proper noun. The condition would falsely claim that 'Abdullah' is not mufrad because 'Abd' signifies something.
But we know 'Abdullah' as a proper noun is mufrad.
3
Understand the author's reasoning
The author argues that signification is based on convention (al-wad‘) and the speaker's intent (qaṣd al-mutalaffiẓ). The positer of the proper noun did not intend 'Abd' to signify when part of 'Abdullah'. Therefore, the part's independent signification is irrelevant.
The condition fails because it ignores the role of convention and intent.
4
Conclude that the condition is erroneous
Thus, the additional condition is invalid. The correct definition of mufrad only requires that no part signifies part of its meaning, not that no part signifies anything at all.

According to the author, why is the additional condition 'no part of a simple utterance may signify anything' a mistake?
A) because all utterances have parts that signify something

Threefold Division: Mufrad, Murakkab, and Mu'allaf
Author presents a threefold classification of utterances based on partial signification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish among mufrad, murakkab, and mu'allaf by applying the criterion of partial signification.

Core Idea
Utterances are classified by whether their parts signify in isolation and/or combination.

In this lesson, we examine a threefold division of utterances proposed by some logicians. The division is based on a simple but precise criterion: does a part of the utterance signify something when considered alone or when combined with others? By understanding this, you will be able to analyze the structure of words and phrases used in Islamic texts, from the Quran to legal terminology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion for classification
The classification is based on 'partial signification' – whether a part of the utterance signifies something. There are two contexts: isolation (when the part stands alone) and combination (when the part is within the whole).
A 'part' here means a meaningful segment, like a word or morpheme, not a single letter.
2
List the four logical possibilities
There are four combinations: (1) part signifies nothing in isolation nor combination, (2) part signifies only in isolation, (3) part signifies in both isolation and combination, (4) part signifies only in combination. The passage states that the fourth is impossible.
Why is the fourth impossible? If a part signifies something only when combined, it would have no independent meaning, so it cannot be a true part of an utterance – it would be like a letter without meaning even in combination.
3
Match each possibility to the three realized types
Possibility (1) is mufrad (simple), e.g., 'asad' (lion) – the letters have no meaning. Possibility (2) is murakkab (compound), e.g., 'Abdullah' as a proper noun – 'abd' and 'Allah' have meaning alone but not as parts of the name. Possibility (3) is mu'allaf (composed), e.g., 'Abdullah' as a descriptive phrase – each word retains its meaning.
Note that the same string 'Abdullah' can be either murakkab or mu'allaf depending on usage.
4
Recognize the majority position
The author notes that most scholars, especially Ibn Sina, do not adopt this threefold division; they treat murakkab broadly as any multi-word expression, covering both murakkab and mu'allaf in this scheme. This nuance helps you understand variability in terminology across Mantiq texts.
When studying later works, be aware that 'murakkab' often includes what is here called 'mu'allaf'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من فرق بين المفرد والمركب، وبين المركب والمؤلف أيضًا؛ فقال الملفوظ في الأربعة التي مرَّ ذكرها من قبل : [1] إما أن لا يدل جزؤه على شيء ما أصلا لا في حالة الانفراد ولا في حالة الاجتماع، وهو المفرد كقولنا: «أسد» مثلا؛ [ب] وإما أن يدل على شيء ما حالة الانفراد فقط، وهو المركب كقولنا : عبد الله إذا كان علمًا؛ [ت] وإما أن يدل على شيء ما حالة الاجتماع والانفراد، وهو المؤلف كقولنا : عبد الله إذا لم يكن علما؛ وإما أن يدل على شيء ما حالة الاجتماع فقط وذلك لا يمكن، غير أنه لا يعتبر عند الأكثر على الخصوص عند الشيخ حسب حمل المركب مقابلا للمفرد.
And among them are those who distinguished between the simple and the compound, and between the compound and the composed as well; they said: the spoken utterance among the four that were mentioned before: [1] either its part does not signify anything at all, neither in isolation nor in combination, and that is the simple, like our saying 'asad' (lion) for example; [b] or it signifies something in isolation only, and that is the compound, like our saying 'Abdullah' when it is a proper noun; [t] or it signifies something in combination and isolation, and that is the composed, like our saying 'Abdullah' when it is not a proper noun; and [th] or it signifies something in combination only, and that is not possible. However, it is not considered by the majority, especially by the Shaykh, according to the interpretation of the compound as opposite to the simple.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage introduces a threefold classification of utterances proposed by some scholars, distinguishing between mufrad (simple), murakkab (compound), and mu'allaf (composed). The classification is based on whether a part of the utterance signifies something in isolation (when the part stands alone) and/or in combination (when the part is within the whole). The author lists four logical possibilities, but only three occur: (1) The part signifies nothing at all—this is the simple, like 'asad' (lion) where the parts 'a', 's', 'd' have no independent meaning; (2) The part signifies something only in isolation, not in combination—this is the compound, like 'Abdullah' as a proper noun, where 'abd' and 'Allah' signify separately but not as parts of the proper noun; (3) The part signifies in both isolation and combination—this is the composed, like 'Abdullah' when used as a descriptive phrase (meaning 'servant of Allah'), where each word retains its meaning. The fourth possibility (signifies only in combination) is deemed impossible. The author notes that this threefold division is not adopted by the majority, especially by the Shaykh (likely Ibn Sina), who instead treats the compound as simply the opposite of the simple, combining both the compound and composed into one category.

المركب (في هذا التقسيم)

Key Takeaway
The classification of utterances into mufrad, murakkab, and mu'allaf depends on whether parts signify in isolation and/or combination; proper nouns are compound while descriptive phrases are composed.

Conventional Nature of Mufrad and Murakkab
Author discusses conventionality of mufrad/murakkab and starts fourfold division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish mufrad from murakkab based on convention (wad‘) and explain why a single utterance can be both depending on context.

Core Idea
Simplicity and composition are not in the letters but in convention.

Some might assume mufrad (simple) and murakkab (compound) are fixed, like a brick versus a wall. But Mantiq scholars teach us that these are matters of convention (wad‘). The same string of letters can be treated as a single unit in one context and as a composition in another. In this lesson, you will learn to see how the classification of utterances depends on how meaning is assigned, not on the physical number of letters.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the objection
The author presents a hypothetical objection: mufrad and murakkab are 'intellectual matters' (umur 'aqliyya), so they seem fixed and cannot change. Notice that this objection treats them as inherent properties of the utterance itself.
2
Identify the author's counter-argument
The author replies that any utterance is always composed of parts (letters), so physical composition cannot be the criterion. Instead, the classification depends on convention (wad‘) and usage (istilah). A single utterance can be mufrad in one conventional setting and murakkab in another.
3
Analyze the example: 'Abdullah'
The author gives the example 'Abdullah'. As a proper noun (a person's name), it is conventionally mufrad—a single label. As a descriptive phrase meaning 'servant of Allah', it is murakkab—two words with their own meanings. Walk through how the same string of letters can be both, depending on how it is used.
This shows that convention, not letters, determines the category.
4
Prepare for the fourfold classification
Having established that utterances and meanings can each be mufrad or murakkab, the author begins to outline a fourfold division: (1) utterance mufrad + meaning mufrad, (2) utterance mufrad + meaning murakkab, (3) utterance murakkab + meaning mufrad, (4) utterance murakkab + meaning murakkab. Though the passage cuts off, you can infer the classification from the context.
The full division is standard in Mantiq texts; the cutting-off is due to OCR.

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection: some might think mufrad and murakkab are fixed intellectual categories that cannot change. The author argues that simplicity or composition is not inherent in the utterance's letters (which are always composed of parts) but depends on convention (wad‘ and istilah). For example, 'Abdullah' can be a proper noun (mufrad in the sense of being a simple label) or a descriptive phrase (murakkab). Thus, an utterance can be mufrad in one context and murakkab in another. The author then begins to outline a fourfold classification of the relationship between utterance (lafz) and meaning (ma‘na): both can be mufrad or murakkab. The passage cuts off, but the implication is that there are four combinations: utterance mufrad and meaning mufrad, utterance mufrad and meaning murakkab, etc. This classification is standard in Mantiq texts.

The Threefold Distinction: Mufrad, Murakkab, Mu'allaf
Author distinguishes mufrad, murakkab, and mu'allaf, then gives fourfold division of word relative to meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mufrad, murakkab, and mu'allaf based on how their parts indicate meaning, and provide an Islamic example for each.

Core Idea
A term's simplicity depends on convention, not just phonetic form.

In logic, words are classified not by their length or letters but by how their parts relate to meaning. This lesson unpacks a threefold distinction among simple (mufrad), compound (murakkab), and composed (mu'allaf) terms, with the key example of 'Abdullah' serving as a case study for how convention can shift a term's classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the property used for classification
Notice that the author distinguishes terms based on whether their parts indicate something about the meaning. This is a functional definition, not a phonetic one.
2
Define mufrad (simple) and its key property
A mufrad term's part indicates nothing at all, neither alone nor in composition. Example: 'lion' – its letters 'l', 'i', 'o', 'n' give no clue to meaning.
The part can be a letter or a syllable.
3
Define murakkab (compound) – part indicates only in isolation
A murakkab term's part indicates something when said alone, but in the whole term it does not. Example: 'Abdullah' as a proper name – 'Abd' alone means 'servant', but in the name it doesn't contribute to meaning.
This is a technical usage; in grammar, murakkab is broader.
4
Define mu'allaf (composed) – part indicates in both situations
A mu'allaf term's part indicates something both in isolation and in composition. Example: 'Abdullah' as a descriptive phrase (servant of Allah) – 'Abd' and 'Allah' each carry meaning alone and together.
This finer distinction is not universally adopted.
5
Test with 'Abdullah' – how convention changes classification
The same word can be murakkab if used as a name, and mu'allaf if used descriptively. This shows that classification depends on convention (wad'), not the word itself.
This answers the objection that simple/compound are fixed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني هو المركب وذلك لا يكون واردا على ما قلناه وإنه – أي المركب- بالنسبة إلى المفرد على التفسير الأول هو الذي يدل على شيء ما حين هو جزؤه، وبالنسبة إليه على التفسير الثاني هو الذي يدل جزؤه على جزء من أجزاء معناه.
ومنهم من فرق بين المفرد والمركب، وبين المركب والمؤلف أيضًا؛ فقال الملفوظ في الأربعة التي مرَّ ذكرها من قبل : [1] إما أن لا يدل جزؤه على شيء ما أصلا لا في حالة الانفراد ولا في حالة الاجتماع، وهو المفرد كقولنا: «أسد» مثلا؛ [ب] وإما أن يدل على شيء ما حالة الانفراد فقط، وهو المركب كقولنا : عبد الله إذا كان علمًا؛ [ت] وإما أن يدل على شيء ما حالة الاجتماع والانفراد، وهو المؤلف كقولنا : عبد الله إذا لم يكن علما؛ وإما أن يدل على شيء ما حالة الاجتماع فقط وذلك لا يمكن، غير أنه لا يعتبر عند الأكثر على الخصوص عند الشيخ حسب حمل المركب مقابلا للمفرد.
ثم لقائل أن يقول المفرد والمركب من الأمور العقلية، فلا يمكن أن ينقلب المفرد مركبا والمركب مفردًا؛ غير أنه لا يمكن بحسب المعاني ولا بحسب الألفاظ، واللفظ مركب من الجزأين فصاعدا لا محالة أي لفظ كان فكونه مفردًا أو مركبًا بحسب الوضع والاصطلاح يمكن أن يكون مفردًا في وضع دون وضع ومركبا كذلك، كما في قولنا: عبد الله» فإنه قد يكون مفردًا وقد يكون مركبا كما مر .
ولما كان اللفظ مفردًا أو مركبا والمعنى كذلك؛ كان اللفظ بالنسبة إلى المعنى على أربعة أقسام؛ وذلك لأنه لا يخلو من أن يكون كل واحد منهما مفردًا كما في اللفظة الدالة على واحد من البسائط مثل لفظة «الجوهر»، أو مركبا في قولنا: العالم حادث وما يجري مجراه؛ أو يكون اللفظ مفردًا دون المعنى كما في «الأسود» إذا كان علمًا لماهية مركبة أو صفة لها ولا يمكن أن يكون على العكس، وهو أن يكون اللفظ مركبا دون المعنى فإنه إذا كان اسما للشيء بجميع أجزائه فهو مفرد، وإذا كان اسما ببعض أجزائه دون البعض، فالبعض منه مهمل والمهمل لا يركب مع المستعمل تركيباً طبيعياً وهذا ظاهر.
ثم لقائل أن يقول كيف هو ؟ وقولنا في تعريف الجوهر موجود لا في موضوع بهذه الصفة، غير أنه لا يكون واردا، والكلام في الاسم لا في الحد والرسم.
The second is the compound, and that is not applicable based on what we said. And it – i.e., the compound – relative to the simple in the first interpretation is that which indicates something when it is a part of it, and relative to it in the second interpretation is that whose part indicates a part of the parts of its meaning. And among them is one who distinguished between the simple and the compound, and also between the compound and the composed; and he said: the uttered word among the four that were previously mentioned: [1] either its part does not indicate anything at all, neither in isolation nor in composition, and it is the simple, like our saying 'lion' for example; [b] or it indicates something only in isolation, and it is the compound, like our saying 'Abdullah' when it is a proper name; [t] or it indicates something in both composition and isolation, and it is the composed, like our saying 'Abdullah' when it is not a proper name; and [th] or it indicates something only in composition, and that is impossible. However, it is not considered by the majority, especially by the Shaykh, according to the carrying of 'compound' as opposed to 'simple'. Then someone may say: the simple and the compound are among intellectual matters, so it is not possible for the simple to become compound nor the compound to become simple, but it is not possible with respect to meanings nor with respect to words. And the word is composed of two parts or more necessarily, whatever word it is. So its being simple or compound is according to convention and terminology; it can be simple in one convention and compound in another, as in our saying 'Abdullah' for it can be simple or compound as passed. And since the word is simple or compound and the meaning likewise, the word in relation to the meaning is of four divisions: that is because it is free from being such that each of them is simple, as in the word indicating one of the simples like the word 'substance'; or both compound, as in our saying 'the world is temporally originated' and what runs its course; or the word is simple without the meaning, as in 'al-Aswad' when it is a proper name for a composite quiddity or an attribute of it; and the opposite is not possible, namely that the word is compound without the meaning. For if it is a name for the thing with all its parts, it is simple; and if it is a name with some of its parts without others, then some of it is futile, and the futile does not combine with the used in a natural composition. This is evident. Then someone may say: how is it? And our saying in the definition of substance 'exists not in a subject' in this manner is not applicable. And the discussion is about the name, not about the definition or description.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies simple and compound terms in logic. It opens by clarifying two interpretations of 'compound' relative to 'simple'. Then it presents a threefold distinction made by some scholars: (1) simple (mufrad) – whose part indicates nothing at all, e.g., 'lion'; (2) compound (murakkab) – whose part indicates something only in isolation, e.g., 'Abdullah' as a proper name; (3) composed (mu'allaf) – whose part indicates in both isolation and composition, e.g., 'Abdullah' when not a proper name. A fourth case (indication only in composition) is deemed impossible. The author notes that this finer distinction is not adopted by the majority, especially by the Shaykh (likely Ibn Sina).

Next, the author addresses an objection: simple and compound are intellectual matters, so they cannot change. But he explains that 'simple' and 'compound' are relative to convention (wad') and terminology (istilah). A word like 'Abdullah' can be simple in one convention (as a proper name) and compound in another (as a descriptive phrase).

Then he gives a fourfold division of word relative to meaning: (1) both simple (e.g., 'substance'), (2) both compound (e.g., 'the world is temporally originated'), (3) word simple but meaning compound (e.g., 'al-Aswad' as a proper name for a composite entity), (4) word compound but meaning simple – impossible because if the word names the whole, it is simple; if it names only parts, the rest is futile and cannot be naturally compounded.

Finally, the author notes that an objection about how definition works is irrelevant here, as the discussion concerns names (alfaz) and not definitions (hadd/rasm). This clarifies the scope of the discussion.

Key Terms
مفرد (mufrad): A term whose part does not indicate any part of its meaning, either in isolation or in composition (e.g., 'lion'). [Note: In later texts, mufrad often means a single word without composition, but here it is defined by the non-indication of its parts.]
مركب (murakkab): A term whose part indicates something only in isolation, e.g., 'Abdullah' as a proper name. [Note: In most logical works, murakkab refers to any expression composed of parts; here it specifically distinguishes between compound and composed.]
مؤلف (mu'allaf): A term whose part indicates something both in isolation and in composition, e.g., 'Abdullah' when not a proper name. [Note: This term is less common; in standard logic, murakkab often covers both murakkab and mu'allaf as defined here.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A term's classification as simple or compound is determined by convention (wad'), not by its phonetic composition.

Fourfold Division of Word and Meaning
Author distinguishes mufrad, murakkab, and mu'allaf, then gives fourfold division of word relative to meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four possible combinations of simple/compound word and simple/compound meaning, and explain why the fourth case is impossible.

Core Idea
A compound word always signals a compound meaning—the reverse is not true.

After classifying terms themselves, the author connects words to their meanings, revealing four logical possibilities. Understanding these helps you analyze how a term relates to its referent in Islamic texts, such as in tafsir and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Recall that both word and meaning can be simple or compound
A word can be simple (mufrad) or compound/composed, and similarly the meaning (ma'na) can be a simple concept or a composite one.
The meaning here is the mental concept, not the external object.
2
Case 1: both word and meaning simple – example 'substance'
The word 'jawhar' (substance) is a single term referring to a simple essence. Its parts (j, w, h, r) indicate nothing about its meaning.
This is the most straightforward case.
3
Case 2: both word and meaning compound – example 'the world is originated'
The sentence 'al-alamu hadithun' (the world is originated) is a compound word (a complete statement) indicating a compound meaning (a judgment).
This covers propositions and complex expressions.
4
Case 3: word simple but meaning compound – example 'al-Aswad' as a proper name
When 'al-Aswad' (The Black) is used as a name for a person, the word is simple (a proper name) but the person's essence is composite (body, soul, attributes).
This shows that simplicity of word does not guarantee simplicity of meaning.
5
Case 4: word compound but meaning simple – why impossible
If a compound word (e.g., 'Abdullah' as a proper name) were to refer to a simple meaning, then either it names the whole (making the word simple) or names only parts (making the rest futile, which cannot form a natural compound).
The author argues that a genuine compound must have all parts contribute to the meaning; if some parts are futile, it's not a true compound.

Source Text

والثاني هو المركب وذلك لا يكون واردا على ما قلناه وإنه – أي المركب- بالنسبة إلى المفرد على التفسير الأول هو الذي يدل على شيء ما حين هو جزؤه، وبالنسبة إليه على التفسير الثاني هو الذي يدل جزؤه على جزء من أجزاء معناه.
ومنهم من فرق بين المفرد والمركب، وبين المركب والمؤلف أيضًا؛ فقال الملفوظ في الأربعة التي مرَّ ذكرها من قبل : [1] إما أن لا يدل جزؤه على شيء ما أصلا لا في حالة الانفراد ولا في حالة الاجتماع، وهو المفرد كقولنا: «أسد» مثلا؛ [ب] وإما أن يدل على شيء ما حالة الانفراد فقط، وهو المركب كقولنا : عبد الله إذا كان علمًا؛ [ت] وإما أن يدل على شيء ما حالة الاجتماع والانفراد، وهو المؤلف كقولنا : عبد الله إذا لم يكن علما؛ وإما أن يدل على شيء ما حالة الاجتماع فقط وذلك لا يمكن، غير أنه لا يعتبر عند الأكثر على الخصوص عند الشيخ حسب حمل المركب مقابلا للمفرد.
ثم لقائل أن يقول المفرد والمركب من الأمور العقلية، فلا يمكن أن ينقلب المفرد مركبا والمركب مفردًا؛ غير أنه لا يمكن بحسب المعاني ولا بحسب الألفاظ، واللفظ مركب من الجزأين فصاعدا لا محالة أي لفظ كان فكونه مفردًا أو مركبًا بحسب الوضع والاصطلاح يمكن أن يكون مفردًا في وضع دون وضع ومركبا كذلك، كما في قولنا: عبد الله» فإنه قد يكون مفردًا وقد يكون مركبا كما مر .
ولما كان اللفظ مفردًا أو مركبا والمعنى كذلك؛ كان اللفظ بالنسبة إلى المعنى على أربعة أقسام؛ وذلك لأنه لا يخلو من أن يكون كل واحد منهما مفردًا كما في اللفظة الدالة على واحد من البسائط مثل لفظة «الجوهر»، أو مركبا في قولنا: العالم حادث وما يجري مجراه؛ أو يكون اللفظ مفردًا دون المعنى كما في «الأسود» إذا كان علمًا لماهية مركبة أو صفة لها ولا يمكن أن يكون على العكس، وهو أن يكون اللفظ مركبا دون المعنى فإنه إذا كان اسما للشيء بجميع أجزائه فهو مفرد، وإذا كان اسما ببعض أجزائه دون البعض، فالبعض منه مهمل والمهمل لا يركب مع المستعمل تركيباً طبيعياً وهذا ظاهر.
ثم لقائل أن يقول كيف هو ؟ وقولنا في تعريف الجوهر موجود لا في موضوع بهذه الصفة، غير أنه لا يكون واردا، والكلام في الاسم لا في الحد والرسم.
The second is the compound, and that is not applicable based on what we said. And it – i.e., the compound – relative to the simple in the first interpretation is that which indicates something when it is a part of it, and relative to it in the second interpretation is that whose part indicates a part of the parts of its meaning. And among them is one who distinguished between the simple and the compound, and also between the compound and the composed; and he said: the uttered word among the four that were previously mentioned: [1] either its part does not indicate anything at all, neither in isolation nor in composition, and it is the simple, like our saying 'lion' for example; [b] or it indicates something only in isolation, and it is the compound, like our saying 'Abdullah' when it is a proper name; [t] or it indicates something in both composition and isolation, and it is the composed, like our saying 'Abdullah' when it is not a proper name; and [th] or it indicates something only in composition, and that is impossible. However, it is not considered by the majority, especially by the Shaykh, according to the carrying of 'compound' as opposed to 'simple'. Then someone may say: the simple and the compound are among intellectual matters, so it is not possible for the simple to become compound nor the compound to become simple, but it is not possible with respect to meanings nor with respect to words. And the word is composed of two parts or more necessarily, whatever word it is. So its being simple or compound is according to convention and terminology; it can be simple in one convention and compound in another, as in our saying 'Abdullah' for it can be simple or compound as passed. And since the word is simple or compound and the meaning likewise, the word in relation to the meaning is of four divisions: that is because it is free from being such that each of them is simple, as in the word indicating one of the simples like the word 'substance'; or both compound, as in our saying 'the world is temporally originated' and what runs its course; or the word is simple without the meaning, as in 'al-Aswad' when it is a proper name for a composite quiddity or an attribute of it; and the opposite is not possible, namely that the word is compound without the meaning. For if it is a name for the thing with all its parts, it is simple; and if it is a name with some of its parts without others, then some of it is futile, and the futile does not combine with the used in a natural composition. This is evident. Then someone may say: how is it? And our saying in the definition of substance 'exists not in a subject' in this manner is not applicable. And the discussion is about the name, not about the definition or description.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies simple and compound terms in logic. It opens by clarifying two interpretations of 'compound' relative to 'simple'. Then it presents a threefold distinction made by some scholars: (1) simple (mufrad) – whose part indicates nothing at all, e.g., 'lion'; (2) compound (murakkab) – whose part indicates something only in isolation, e.g., 'Abdullah' as a proper name; (3) composed (mu'allaf) – whose part indicates in both isolation and composition, e.g., 'Abdullah' when not a proper name. A fourth case (indication only in composition) is deemed impossible. The author notes that this finer distinction is not adopted by the majority, especially by the Shaykh (likely Ibn Sina).

Next, the author addresses an objection: simple and compound are intellectual matters, so they cannot change. But he explains that 'simple' and 'compound' are relative to convention (wad') and terminology (istilah). A word like 'Abdullah' can be simple in one convention (as a proper name) and compound in another (as a descriptive phrase).

Then he gives a fourfold division of word relative to meaning: (1) both simple (e.g., 'substance'), (2) both compound (e.g., 'the world is temporally originated'), (3) word simple but meaning compound (e.g., 'al-Aswad' as a proper name for a composite entity), (4) word compound but meaning simple – impossible because if the word names the whole, it is simple; if it names only parts, the rest is futile and cannot be naturally compounded.

Finally, the author notes that an objection about how definition works is irrelevant here, as the discussion concerns names (alfaz) and not definitions (hadd/rasm). This clarifies the scope of the discussion.

Key Terms
مفرد (mufrad): A term whose part does not indicate any part of its meaning, either in isolation or in composition (e.g., 'lion'). [Note: In later texts, mufrad often means a single word without composition, but here it is defined by the non-indication of its parts.]
مركب (murakkab): A term whose part indicates something only in isolation, e.g., 'Abdullah' as a proper name. [Note: In most logical works, murakkab refers to any expression composed of parts; here it specifically distinguishes between compound and composed.]
مؤلف (mu'allaf): A term whose part indicates something both in isolation and in composition, e.g., 'Abdullah' when not a proper name. [Note: This term is less common; in standard logic, murakkab often covers both murakkab and mu'allaf as defined here.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which case is impossible?
A) Simple word, simple meaning
B) Simple word, compound meaning
C) Compound word, compound meaning
D) Compound word, simple meaning

Key Takeaway
The relationship between word and meaning is not one-to-one; a simple word can signify a complex meaning, but a compound word must signify a complex meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the phrase 'bismillah' (In the name of Allah). Is it a simple or compound word? Is its meaning simple or compound? Which of the four cases does it fall into? Explain.

Critique of the 'Independent Concept' Definition of the Noun
Author critiques the first definition of the noun: independent in concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the definition 'the noun is a word indicating what is independent in concept' fails and which word types it mistakenly includes.

Core Idea
A definition that includes particles is too broad for the noun.

In logic and grammar, defining the noun is notoriously tricky. The author examines a common definition—'independent in concept'—and shows that it inadvertently includes particles, which are a separate part of speech. Understanding this critique trains you to spot overbroad definitions.

Guided Path
1
State the definition
The definition given is: 'The noun is a word indicating what is independent in concept without indicating time.' Write it down.
2
Test against particles
Consider a particle like 'fi' (in). Its concept (being inside) is independent—you can conceive it without needing another word. So the definition would include particles, which are not nouns.
Particles are a separate part of speech in Arabic grammar.
3
Examine the author's argument modalities
The author uses a reductio: if particles were not independent in concept, they would need to be independent in accepting the judgment of non-independence, leading to contradiction. Follow this reasoning step by step.
This shows the definition leads to absurdity.
4
Consider counterarguments
Even if we grant that particles are non-independent, relative pronouns and demonstratives are also non-independent yet are nouns. So the definition fails both ways.
5
Note the Kufan objection
Some Kufans deny that these pronouns are nouns. But the author says the discussion is with others. This shows the definition is debated even among grammarians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الاسم من جملة ما يعرف بالتعريفات المختلفة، كما قيل: «الاسم كلمة دالة على ما هو المستقل في المفهومية من غير أن يدل على زمان ذلك المعنى؛ وفيه من الكلام فإن الحروف كلها كذلك لما أن المفهوم من الحرف أي حرف كان مستقلاً في المفهومية، وإلا لا يمكن أن تكون مفهومات الحروف متصورة يمكن تركيب منها ومن غيرها. ولأنه إذا لم يكن مستقلا في المفهومية كان مستقلًا لقبول هذا الحكم وهو كونه غير مستقل، والاستقلال لقبول الاستقلالية استقلال فيكون مستقلا في التصور؛ ولو كان مستقلا فلا يصح أن يقال إنه غير مستقل، وإن سلمنا بأنه غير مستقل لكن اللفظ «الذي» وما يجرى مجراه من الموصولات والمضمرات والمنبهات مفهوماتها غير مستقل، وإن كانت الجملة بجميع أفرادها من الأسماء. وإن قيل هذا في حيز المنع لما أنها لا تكون من الأسماء عند البعض من أهل الكوفة، فنقول: كلامنا في هذا الباب مع الغير، وقد قيل في تعريف الاسم أيضًا: إنه كلمة تستحق الإعراب في أول موضوعها من الرسوم العائدة كذلك». وقد كان من الأسماء ما لا يكون كذلك في نفسه كالمضمرات ونحوها، فإن إليها في قولنا إنه مثلا لا يستحق الإعراب أول موضوعها. وأما قول صاحب الكشاف الاسم ما دل على معنى في نفسه دلالة مجردة عن الاقتران بالزمان»، فإن فيه من الكلام أيضًا؛ وقد قيل فيه: «إن الحط والعقد والإشارة كلها كذلك»، وأنها ليست من الأسماء.
Then the noun is among things known by various definitions, as it was said: 'The noun is a word indicating what is independent in concept without indicating the time of that meaning.' There is discussion in this: because all particles are like that, since the concept of any particle is independent in concept; otherwise the concepts of particles could not be conceived and composed with others. And because if it was not independent in concept, it would be independent in accepting this judgment of being non-independent, and independence in accepting independence is independence, so it is independent in conception. And if it was independent, it cannot be said that it is non-independent. And even if we grant that it is non-independent, the word 'alladhi' and similar relative pronouns, implicit pronouns, and demonstratives have non-independent concepts, even though the sentence in all its individuals is from the nouns. And if it is said that this is subject to denial because they are not nouns according to some of the Kufan scholars, we say: our discussion here is with others. And it has also been said in the definition of the noun: 'It is a word that deserves inflection in its first position from the definitions that return.' And there are nouns that are not like that in themselves, like implicit pronouns, etc., for example 'ila' in our saying 'innahu' does not deserve inflection in its first position. As for the statement of Sahib al-Kashshaf: 'The noun is what indicates a meaning in its own indication abstracted from association with time,' there is discussion in it as well. And it was said: 'Al-hatt, al-'aqd, and al-ishara are all like that,' and they are not from the nouns.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques two definitions of the noun. The author first quotes the definition: 'The noun is a word indicating what is independent in concept without indicating the time of that meaning.' He then points out a problem: particles (huruf) also have independent concepts, as they can be conceived and combined with others. He argues that if they were not independent, they would have to be independent in accepting the judgment of non-independence, leading to a contradiction. Even if we concede they are non-independent, relative pronouns and demonstratives have non-independent concepts yet they are considered nouns by some. He then addresses an objection that Kufan scholars do not consider these as nouns, but the author says the discussion is with others. Next, he cites another definition: 'The noun is a word that deserves inflection in its first position.' But he notes that pronouns like 'ila' do not deserve inflection in their first position. Finally, he criticizes the definition from al-Kashshaf: 'The noun is what indicates a meaning in its own indication abstracted from association with time.' He objects that gestures, nods, and signs also indicate meaning abstracted from time, yet they are not nouns. This demonstrates the difficulty of defining the noun accurately and the author's critical approach to existing definitions.

Key Terms
الاستقلال في المفهومية
الموصولات
المضمرات
المنبهات

Key Takeaway
Overbroad definitions can be refuted by finding counterexamples from the same linguistic category.

Critique of the Inflection-Based and Abstract-Meaning Definitions of the Noun
Author critiques inflection-based and abstract-meaning definitions of the noun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why defining the noun by 'deserving inflection' or by 'meaning in itself abstracted from time' is inadequate.

Core Idea
Definitions based on inflection or abstract meaning fail because pronouns don't inflect initially and gestures are not nouns.

The author continues his critical survey of noun definitions. He examines a definition based on inflection (i'rab) and another from al-Kashshaf based on abstract meaning. Both are shown to have exceptions—pronouns that don't inflect initially, and gestures that indicate meaning. This sharpens our understanding of what a proper definition requires.

Guided Path
1
State the second definition
The definition is: 'The noun is a word that deserves inflection in its first position.' Write it down.
2
Find the counterexample
Pronouns like 'huwa' or 'ila' in 'innahu' do not take inflection in their first position (they are built). Thus, by this definition, they would not be nouns, but they are.
3
State the Kashshaf definition
Al-Kashshaf's definition: 'The noun is what indicates a meaning in itself abstracted from time.'
4
Find the counterexample
Gestures (hatt, 'aqd, ishara) also indicate meaning in themselves without time, but they are not nouns.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Both definitions are either too narrow or too broad. A correct definition must include all nouns and exclude non-nouns.

Classification of Nouns in Arabic: Muhassal, Ghayr Muhassal, Munsarif, Ghayr Munsarif
Author categorizes nouns into concrete and abstract, and inflectable and non-inflectable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any Arabic noun as concrete (muhassal) or non-concrete (ghayr muhassal), and as inflectable (munsarif) or non-inflectable (ghayr munsarif), based on the criteria given in the text.

Core Idea
Nouns are categorized by concreteness and inflection; these two axes determine grammatical behavior.

In this lesson, we explore the author's classification of nouns into concrete (muhassal) and non-concrete (ghayr muhassal), and then into inflectable (munsarif) and non-inflectable (ghayr munsarif). This distinction is fundamental for understanding how nouns function in Arabic grammar, especially regarding case endings and the concept of additions (zawa'id). We will see that even negated indefinite nouns like 'la insan' are considered nouns, though they lack concrete reference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the noun's reference
Determine if the noun refers to an actual entity (e.g., human, horse) or is a negated indefinite (e.g., 'no human'). The former is muhassal, the latter ghayr muhassal.
2
Check for inflection
See if the noun accepts case endings (i'rab) and tanwin. If it does, it is munsarif; if not (e.g., proper nouns like 'Ibrahim'), it is ghayr munsarif, also called qaim jamid.
3
Distinguish phonetic vs. non-phonetic additions
Note that some additions are heard (like damma, fatha) and some are not (like abstract grammatical function). Inflection belongs to the non-phonetic category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الاسم إما محصل مثل الإنسان والفرس والشجر والحجر ونحو ذلك، وإما غير محصل مثل لا إنسان ولا بصير ولا عادل ولا كريم مثلا. فإنه يعد من الأسماء وإن لم يكن بالحقيقة منها كما مر ، وكل واحد منها إما منصرف [١٤ و] وهو الذي يلحقه ويقترن به ما يشير إلى منع غير ما يشير إليه / مجرد الاسم، وذلك من الحركات والأعاريب حتى إن المسموع مجموع حاصل من الاسم وما يلحقه ; وإما غير منصرف وهو الذي لا يلحقه من الزوائد الدالة على غير معناه، وهذا هو المسمى بالقائم جامد. ثم ما يلحق الاسم من الزوائد التي مرَّ ذكرها; فإما أن يكون بحسب اللفظ ، وإما أن لا يكون; وما لا يكون بحسب اللفظ فذلك لا يمكن أن يكون مسموعا، والإعراب من تلك الجملة كذلك.
Then the noun is either concrete, like 'insan' (human), 'faras' (horse), 'shajar' (tree), 'hajar' (stone), etc., or non-concrete, like 'la insan' (no human), 'la basir' (no seeing), 'la 'adil' (no just), 'la karim' (no generous), etc. For it is counted among nouns even if it is not truly one, as mentioned. And each of them is either inflectable [14 w] — which is that which has attached to it and associated with it what indicates preventing other than what the bare noun indicates, and that is through movements and inflections, so that what is heard is a composite resulting from the noun and what attaches to it — or non-inflectable, which is that which does not have attached to it the additions indicating other than its meaning, and this is what is called 'qaim jamid' (standing solid). Then the additions that attach to the noun, as mentioned, are either phonetic or not; and what is not phonetic cannot be heard, and inflection is among that category as well.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage classifies nouns into two primary divisions: muhassal (concrete) and ghayr muhassal (non-concrete). Concrete nouns refer to tangible entities like human, horse, tree, stone. Non-concrete nouns include negated indefinite nouns like 'la insan' (no human), which are still considered nouns though not truly concrete. Each noun then is either munsarif (inflectable) or ghayr munsarif (non-inflectable). An inflectable noun takes case endings (i'rab) that indicate grammatical relations beyond the bare meaning. A non-inflectable noun does not accept such endings; it is called 'qaim jamid' (standing solid). The author then discusses the 'additions' (zawa'id) that attach to the noun: some are phonetic (like case endings) and some are non-phonetic (like the concept of inflection itself). This highlights the difference between audible markers (like damma, fatha) and the abstract grammatical function. The classification is important for understanding how nouns function in Arabic grammar and logic, particularly in distinguishing between what can receive inflection and what cannot.

Key Terms
محصل
غير محصل
منصرف
غير منصرف
قائم جامد
الزوائد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a non-concrete (ghayr muhassal) noun?
A) 'رجل' (man)
B) 'لا إنسان' (no human)
C) 'كتاب' (book)
D) 'شمس' (sun)

Key Takeaway
Arabic nouns are classified by concreteness and inflection, with non-concrete nouns (like negated indefinites) counted as nouns, and inflectable nouns taking case endings that are either phonetic or non-phonetic.

Critique of Zamakhshari's Definition of Ism
Critique of Zamakhshari's definition of ism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaw in al-Zamakhshari's definition of ism and distinguish true nouns from particles that indicate meaning independently.

Core Idea
A definition that includes particles like al-hatt is too broad; a noun must also accept inflectional markers.

The passage begins by noting that some words function as nouns without being nouns in themselves, such as pronouns. Then it turns to a famous definition of 'ism' from al-Kashshaf and shows why it fails. Understanding this critique sharpens your ability to evaluate definitions in both grammar and logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition under critique
Find Zamakhshari's definition: 'The noun is what indicates a meaning in itself, an indication stripped of association with time.'
2
Find the counterexamples provided
The author mentions al-hatt, al-'aqd, and al-ishara as also indicating meaning in themselves without time, yet they are not nouns.
These are particles or gestures, not nouns.
3
Identify the error in the definition
The definition is too broad (ghayr mani'): it includes things that are not nouns.
4
Understand the implied correction
A proper definition must include that nouns are subject to grammatical inflection (i'rāb) and other noun-specific features.
The passage later discusses i'rāb as a non-phonetic addition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان من الأسماء ما لا يكون كذلك في نفسه كالمضمرات ونحوها، فإن إليها في قولنا إنه مثلا لا يستحق الإعراب أول موضوعها. وأما قول صاحب الكشاف الاسم ما دل على معنى في نفسه دلالة مجردة عن الاقتران بالزمان»، فإن فيه من الكلام أيضًا؛ وقد قيل فيه: «إن الحط والعقد والإشارة كلها كذلك»، وأنها ليست من الأسماء. ثم الاسم إما محصل مثل الإنسان والفرس والشجر والحجر ونحو ذلك، وإما غير محصل مثل لا إنسان ولا بصير ولا عادل ولا كريم مثلا. فإنه يعد من الأسماء وإن لم يكن بالحقيقة منها كما مر، وكل واحد منها إما منصرف وهو الذي يلحقه ويقترن به ما يشير إلى منع غير ما يشير إليه مجرد الاسم، وذلك من الحركات والأعاريب حتى إن المسموع مجموع حاصل من الاسم وما يلحقه؛ وإما غير منصرف وهو الذي لا يلحقه من الزوائد الدالة على غير معناه، وهذا هو المسمى بالقائم جامد. ثم ما يلحق الاسم من الزوائد التي مرَّ ذكرها؛ فإما أن يكون بحسب اللفظ، وإما أن لا يكون؛ وما لا يكون بحسب اللفظ فذلك لا يمكن أن يكون مسموعا، والإعراب من تلك الجملة كذلك.
Among the nouns (asma') there are those that are not [nouns] in themselves, like pronouns and the like, for example 'hi' (إليها) in our saying 'innahu' does not deserve i'rab as its first subject. As for the statement of Sahib al-Kashshaf: 'The noun is what indicates a meaning in itself, an indication stripped of association with time', there is also speech about it; and it has been said concerning it: 'Indeed, al-hatt, al-'aqd, and al-ishara are all like that [i.e., they indicate a meaning in themselves without time], and they are not nouns.' Then the noun is either concrete (muḥṣal) like 'man', 'horse', 'tree', 'stone' and such, or non-concrete (ghayr muḥṣal) like 'no man', 'no seer', 'no just', 'no generous' for example. For it is counted among the nouns even if it is not in reality from them as has passed. And each of them is either declinable (munṣarif) — which is that which attaches to it and is accompanied by what indicates the prevention of other than what the bare noun indicates, that being from the harakat and i'rab until what is heard is a composite of the noun and what attaches to it — or non-declinable (ghayr munṣarif) — which is that to which none of the additions indicating other than its meaning attach, and this is what is called 'permanent/rigid' (qa'im jamid). Then what attaches to the noun of the additions mentioned: it is either according to the utterance or not; and what is not according to the utterance cannot be heard, and i'rab is likewise from that category.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage begins with a subtlety: some words that function as nouns in practice are not nouns in themselves, such as pronouns (ḍamā'ir). The author points out that a pronoun like 'hi' (إليها) does not deserve i'rab as a subject because it is not truly a noun. Then the author critiques the definition of 'noun' given by the author of al-Kashshaf (Zamakhshari): 'the noun is what indicates a meaning in itself, an indication stripped of association with time.' The objection raised is that other categories like particles of depreciation (al-hatt), contract (al-'aqd), and pointing (al-ishara) also indicate a meaning in themselves without time, yet they are not considered nouns. Thus the definition is too broad. Next, the author classifies nouns into two types: concrete (muḥṣal) such as 'man', 'horse', etc., and non-concrete (ghayr muḥṣal) such as 'no man' (لا إنسان) which are considered nouns by convention though not by reality. Then each type is further divided into declinable (munṣarif) and non-declinable (ghayr munṣarif). Declinable nouns accept inflectional endings (ḥarakāt and i'rāb) that modify the meaning, while non-declinable nouns do not, and are called 'rigid' (jamid). The author then notes that additions to the noun are either phonetic (related to pronunciation) or non-phonetic; and i'rāb is among the non-phonetic additions because it is not heard as a separate sound. The discussion is definitional, clarifying what qualifies as a noun in Arabic grammar and logic, and rejecting a flawed definition.

Key Terms
اسم (ism): A word that indicates a meaning in itself without indication of time; includes concrete and non-concrete nouns [Note: In later logic texts, ism may be restricted to universal terms; here it includes grammatical nouns.]
محصل (muḥṣal): Concrete noun referring to a specific entity like 'man' or 'horse'
غير محصل (ghayr muḥṣal): Abstract or indefinite noun like 'no man' (لا إنسان), considered a noun by convention
منصرف (munṣarif): Declinable noun that accepts full inflection (i'rāb) and tanwīn
غير منصرف (ghayr munṣarif): Non-declinable noun that does not accept tanwīn or full kasra

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject al-Zamakhshari's definition of ism?
A) Because it fails to include pronouns.
B) Because it also applies to particles like al-hatt which are not nouns.
C) Because it is too narrow.
D) Because it mentions time.

Key Takeaway
A proper definition of 'ism' must exclude particles that indicate meaning without time, ensuring it is not overly broad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Muḥṣal and Ghayr Muḥṣal: Concrete and Non-Concrete Nouns
Classification of nouns into muḥṣal and ghayr muḥṣal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize Arabic nouns as muḥṣal or ghayr muḥṣal and explain the logical distinction between them.

Core Idea
Ghayr muḥṣal nouns are nouns by convention, not by reality.

After defining ism in general, the author divides nouns into two broad categories: muḥṣal (concrete) and ghayr muḥṣal (non-concrete). This classification is important for understanding how terms are used in logical and grammatical contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify examples of muḥṣal nouns
The author gives examples: 'man', 'horse', 'tree', 'stone'. These refer to concrete entities.
Muḥṣal nouns denote specific, determinate referents.
2
Identify examples of ghayr muḥṣal nouns
Examples: 'no man', 'no seer', 'no just', 'no generous'. These are indefinite or negated expressions.
Note the construction 'lā + noun' is considered a noun despite denoting negation.
3
Understand the author's remark on ghayr muḥṣal
The author says these are counted among nouns even if not in reality. This signals a conventional usage.
In logic, ghayr muḥṣal terms are often called 'privative' or 'indefinite'.
4
Apply the distinction to new examples
Given a noun like 'non-believer' (kāfir), determine if it is muḥṣal or ghayr muḥṣal.
Think: does it denote a concrete entity or is it a negation?

Declinable and Non-Declinable Nouns (Munṣarif and Ghayr Munṣarif)
Classification of nouns into munṣarif and ghayr munṣarif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between munṣarif and ghayr munṣarif nouns and identify their inflectional characteristics.

Core Idea
Munṣarif nouns accept tanwīn and full case endings; ghayr munṣarif are rigid and limited in inflection.

The passage further divides each type of noun into declinable (munṣarif) and non-declinable (ghayr munṣarif). This distinction is crucial for proper pronunciation and understanding of Arabic grammar, especially in reading the Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
Define munṣarif
Munṣarif nouns accept the full range of inflectional endings (ḥarakāt and i'rāb) that modify meaning, such as tanwīn and case vowels.
Example: baytun (nominative), baytin (genitive), baytan (accusative).
2
Define ghayr munṣarif
Ghayr munṣarif nouns do not accept tanwīn and have limited case endings (e.g., no kasra in the genitive). They are called 'jamid' (rigid).
Example: Aḥmadu (nominative), Aḥmada (accusative/genitive) - no tanwīn, and no kasra in genitive.
3
Understand the author's note about additions
The author mentions that some additions to nouns are phonetic (like tanwīn) and some are non-phonetic (like i'rāb). I'rāb is non-phonetic, so it is not heard as a separate sound.
This explains why i'rāb is often not written in unvocalized texts.
4
Apply the distinction
Given a noun, determine if it is munṣarif or ghayr munṣarif and state the reason.
Look for characteristics like being a proper noun, having a feminine ending, or being a foreign word, which often cause non-declinability.

Source Text

Next, the author classifies nouns into two types: concrete (muḥṣal) such as 'man', 'horse', etc., and non-concrete (ghayr muḥṣal) such as 'no man' (لا إنسان) which are considered nouns by convention though not by reality. Then each type is further divided into declinable (munṣarif) and non-declinable (ghayr munṣarif). Declinable nouns accept inflectional endings (ḥarakāt and i'rāb) that modify the meaning, while non-declinable nouns do not, and are called 'rigid' (jamid). The author then notes that additions to the noun are either phonetic (related to pronunciation) or non-phonetic; and i'rāb is among the non-phonetic additions because it is not heard as a separate sound. The discussion is definitional, clarifying what qualifies as a noun in Arabic grammar and logic, and rejecting a flawed definition.

Key Terms
اسم (ism): A word that indicates a meaning in itself without indication of time; includes concrete and non-concrete nouns [Note: In later logic texts, ism may be restricted to universal terms; here it includes grammatical nouns.]
محصل (muḥṣal): Concrete noun referring to a specific entity like 'man' or 'horse'
غير محصل (ghayr muḥṣal): Abstract or indefinite noun like 'no man' (لا إنسان), considered a noun by convention
منصرف (munṣarif): Declinable noun that accepts full inflection (i'rāb) and tanwīn
غير منصرف (ghayr munṣarif): Non-declinable noun that does not accept tanwīn or full kasra

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes a ghayr munṣarif noun from a munṣarif noun?
A) It indicates time.
B) It does not accept tanwīn and has limited case endings.
C) It is always a proper noun.
D) It cannot be used in the nominative.

Key Takeaway
Munṣarif nouns accept full inflection including tanwīn; ghayr munṣarif nouns are rigid and reject tanwīn, following special declension rules.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Logical Definition of Kalima (Verb)
Author defines kalima (verb) as a word indicating an event and its time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the genus and differentia from the definition of kalima and assess its completeness as a logical definition.

Core Idea
A verb is not just a word – it is a word that points to both an event and its time.

In classical logic, the parts of speech are defined with precision. The definition of kalima (verb) given here is a model of logical definition: it isolates the essential features (genus and differentia) to distinguish the verb from nouns and other utterances. In this lesson, we will dissect that definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definiendum in the Arabic. The passage begins by stating 'The kalima is...' (والكلمة هي اللفظة المفرد المستقلة...).
2
Extract the genus
What broader category does the kalima belong to? The genus is 'single independent utterance' (اللفظة المفرد المستقلة). This places it among the parts of speech.
3
Identify the differentia
What distinguishes the kalima from other utterances like nouns? The differentia is 'indicating something and the time of its existence' (الدالة بالتواطؤ على شيء وعلى زمان وجوده). The author explicitly says 'and the time of its existence' is to exclude nouns.
4
Test completeness
Does the definition include all verbs and exclude non-verbs? Check: verbal nouns like 'ḍarb' are excluded because they don't indicate time; derived nouns like 'ḍārib' are also excluded. The author addresses potential counterexamples.
5
Compare hadd vs rasm
Is this a real definition (hadd) or a description (rasm)? It seems to be a complete definition because it includes the essential differentia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني منها حالة تدل على الفاعلية أو المفعولية داماد است فذلك في الكلمة، والكلمة هي اللفظة المفرد المستقلة الدالة بالتواطؤ على شيء وعلى زمان وجوده كذلك. أما فائدة اللفظ والمفردة والمستقلة والدالة بالتواطؤ فقد مرَّ ذكرها من قبل، وأما قوله وعلى زمان وجوده فذلك لإخراج الأسماء، وقوله : «كذلك» يشعر بأنه يدل على الزمان مما يدل على غيره بحسب جوهر اللفظ فتكون الكلمة دالة على شيء وعلى زمان يقترن به فقولنا «ضرب» يدل على ضرب موجود في زمان من الأزمنة الثلاثة على خلاف الضرب، فإنه وإن دل على شيء ما فلا يدل على زمان يقترن به من الأزمنة الثلاثة . ثم الكلمة عند النحاة يسمى فعلا لما أنه يدل على الفعل وهو المصدر مما يدل عليه وعلى وجوده في الماضي يسمى «الماضي»، وما يدل عليه وعلى وجوده في المستقبل يسمى «المستقبل»، وما يدل عليه وعلى وجوده في الحال يسمى «الأمر » ، ولا يلزم عليه ضارب وذاهب مثلا لما أنهما من الأسماء المشتقة ولا كلام فيها بل الكلام في الكلمات؛ فالماضي منها لا يراد به الحال إلا بطريق المجاز. وكذلك المستقبل فإنك إذا قلت : «زيد يمشي فلا يفهم منه المشي في الحال إلا بزيادة شأن، كما إذا قلت: «زيد هو ذا يمشي ، أو قلت المشيبي وما يتعين به المشي في الاستقبال. فذلك حرف السين أو «سو» مع غيره ، كما في قولك : «سيمشي» أو «سو يمشي» أو «سف يمشي» أو «سوف» على الترتيب المذكور الأقرب فالأقرب. وإن قيل : كيف هو وقد نقل عن الثقات أنه مشترك بين الحال والاستقبال ؟ فنقول : نقل عن الثقات الطعن في هذا القول كذلك اللهم إلا أن يقال المعنى المشترك في هذا الموضع أن يستعمل الحال مرة والاستقبال أخرى. وذلك من جملة ما لا نزاع فيه بل النزاع في كونه مشتركًا بحسب الوضع يعني وضع في أصل الوضع لهذا. ولذلك كذلك حتى إذا استعمل في كل منهما، فإنه يستعمل بطريق الحقيقة، وكما أن الاسم من جملة ما يعرف بالتعريفات الفاسدة فكذلك الكلمة. وقد نقل عن سيبويه في الأفعال أنها أمثلة أخذت من لفظ أحداث الأسماء، وأنه في مقام التعريف باطل لما أنه لا يعرف منه الفعل ولأنه ينقض بالألفاظ الدالة على الفاعلين والمفعولين.
And as for the second of them, a state indicating agentivity or objecthood — that is in the word (kalima). And the kalima is the single, independent utterance which conventionally indicates something and also the time of its existence. As for the utility of 'utterance', 'single', 'independent', and 'conventionally indicating' has already been mentioned. And as for his saying 'and the time of its existence', that is to exclude nouns. And his saying 'also' (kadhālika) suggests that it indicates time in addition to indicating something else according to the essence of the utterance. So the kalima indicates a thing and a time associated with it. Thus our saying 'ḍaraba' indicates a hitting existing in one of the three times, unlike 'ḍarb' (the verbal noun), for although it indicates something, it does not indicate a time associated with it from the three times. Then the kalima according to grammarians is called 'fi'l' (verb) because it indicates an action (fi'l) which is the verbal noun: what indicates it and its existence in the past is called 'māḍī' (past), what indicates it and its existence in the future is called 'mustaqbal' (future), and what indicates it and its existence in the present is called 'amr' (imperative). And it is not refuted by examples like ḍārib (striker) and dhāhib (goer) because they are derived nouns, not verbs. So the past tense is not used for the present except metaphorically. Similarly the future: if you say 'Zayd yamshī' (Zayd walks), walking in the present is not understood except with an extra matter, as when you say 'Zayd huwa dhā yamshī' (Zayd is walking right now). And what definitively indicates walking in the future is the letter sīn or sawfa, as in 'sayamshī' or 'saw yamshī' or 'saf yamshī' or 'sawfa', in the order mentioned from nearest to farthest. And if it is said: 'How is that when it has been transmitted from reliable authorities that it (the present tense) is shared (mushtarak) between present and future?' We say: It has been transmitted from reliable authorities the criticism of this statement as well. Except that it may be said that the shared meaning in this context is that it is used sometimes for present and sometimes for future. That is among what there is no dispute about, but the dispute is about its being shared according to the original lexical placement. Therefore, even if used in both, it is used in a real sense. And just as the noun is among what is known by flawed definitions, so too the kalima. And it has been transmitted from Sībawayh concerning verbs that they are patterns taken from the utterance of events (aḥdāth) of nouns, and that in the context of definition it is false because the verb is not known from it and because it is refuted by words indicating agents and objects.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines the kalima (often translated as 'verb' in logic texts, but more precisely 'word' that indicates an event with time). The author first states that the second category (after nouns) is the 'state indicating agentivity or objecthood'—this is a reference to the verbal derivatives? Actually, 'the second' refers to the second part of speech (after ism). The kalima is defined as an independent, single utterance that conventionally indicates a thing and also the time of its existence. The author explains that 'time of its existence' excludes nouns like 'ḍarb' (striking), which indicate an event but not a specific time. Then he aligns with Arabic grammarians: the kalima is called fi'l (verb) because it indicates an action (al-fi'l is the verbal noun); the three tenses: māḍī (past), mustaqbal (future), and amr (imperative). He notes that derived nouns like 'ḍārib' (striker) are not verbs and do not refute this. He then discusses usage: the past cannot indicate present except metaphorically; the present can indicate future, but with particles like sīn or sawfa to clarify. An objection arises: reliable grammarians say the present tense is shared (mushtarak) between present and future. The response: the 'sharing' refers to usage, not lexical ambiguity; there is no dispute that it can be used for both, but the dispute is about original placement. He ends by noting that just as nouns have flawed definitions, so do verbs, citing Sībawayh's definition that verbs are patterns taken from nouns—this is rejected because it does not identify the essential nature of the verb and is open to counterexamples like agent/object nouns.

Key Terms
كلمة
لفظة مفردة مستقلة
زمان الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To include nouns
B) To exclude nouns
C) To indicate the future
D) To show convention

Key Takeaway
The definition of kalima in logic is a paradigmatic hadd: genus (independent utterance) + differentia (indicates thing and time).

The Three Tenses of the Verb (Māḍī, Mustaqbal, Amr)
Author explains the three tenses of the verb and the function of future markers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the tense of any Arabic verb example using the indicators discussed in the passage and explain the logical basis for the present tense's flexibility.

Core Idea
Arabic verbs encode time inherently, but the present tense requires context or particles to pinpoint the future.

After defining the kalima, the author explains its tenses: past, future, and imperative. He emphasizes that the present tense (used for both present and future) is not lexically ambiguous but context-dependent. This lesson guides you through the logical indicators of each tense.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three tenses
The author lists māḍī (past) for past existence, mustaqbal (future) for future existence, and amr (imperative) for present existence. These three cover the times.
Note: the imperative is considered present tense in Arabic grammar.
2
Understand the present tense's dual use
The present tense (e.g., yamshī) can indicate present or future. The author states that without extra context, it is not limited to present.
3
Recognize future markers
The author lists particles: sīn, sawfa, etc., that definitively indicate future. For example, 'sayamshī' means 'he will walk'.
4
Assess the objection about shared meaning
Some grammarians say the present tense is shared (mushtarak) between present and future. The author responds that it is shared in usage, not original lexical placement.
This is a key logical distinction: ambiguity vs. contextual flexibility.
